I
==PAGE:3==
Abstract
This thesis examines the integration of play into the urban environment and its impact on livability in the 
city of Copenhagen, particularly for children. Several strategies for designing play spaces in cities, includ­
ing traditional playgrounds, public parks and squares, and city interiors, were evaluated for their impact on 
social interaction, child development, and community engagement.
In partnership with Gehl Architects, human behavior in the city was observed using their tools and met­
hods. The study also delves into the challenges of incorporating the proposed solution, the Interactive Bo­
ard, into densely populated areas such as limited space, safety, and conflicting public space use. Through a 
bottom-up approach, the research analyses how to successfully design a solution that promotes play and 
social interaction among different age groups and communities, making it inclusive and accessible.
The conclusion highlights the importance of play in urban environments and suggests three design pro­
posals for the Interactive Board, as a means of enhancing the quality of life for its inhabitants. The solution 
aims to promote play by intervening on the curbside to help shape the play culture in the urban environ­
ment.

INTRODUCTION
==PAGE:10==
Integrate play into the urban environment of Copenhagen
01
Introduction
01
1.1 Motivation
The growth in the global population and the resultant concentration of people in urban areas has led to an 
increased number of children being raised in urban environments. Based on current trends, approximately 
70 percent of the global population will live in urban areas, and a majority of these citizens will be under 
the age of 18 years old (Weedy, 2020). As a result of the rapid growth, the children’s lives, health, and future 
will be determined by urban development in the coming decades. It is crucial to understand the needs and 
demands of the new inhabitants, the children, to be prepared to adapt to them. 
The need that this project emphasizes is children’s play and the developmental gains that come from 
playing in the city. Play is an important aspect of children’s development, both in the areas of motor skills, 
cognitive learning, and social understanding. Through play, it is possible to create a gathering point for 
social acquaintances, as play can help break down the barriers between different groups of people across 
identity, race, language barriers, social class, and whatever else can create division between people (Scar-
lett et al., 2005). The increased social engagement can help create connection, well-being, and cohesion 
within a local community (Innocent & Stevens, 2021).
The aim of the project is to bring play out of the playground to encourage more momentarily, playful 
interactions for children in the urban environment. Thus, hoping to encourage exploration and social en-
counters and to break the barriers that create division within the community. The integration of play is to 
be used as a means of creating an attachment and belonging to the city for the children. Hopefully, the 
children’s participation in and interaction with the city help foster a change in the general play culture, in-
spiring communities to gather in the hopes of increasing the general playfulness of the city and, as a direct 
consequence increasing feelings of safety and well-being in an ever-expanding city.
1.2 Gehl - Project Partner 
Over the last several years, Gehl Architects has had an increased focus on children-centered urban plan-
ning and development, leading to growing expertise in designing cities that supports children (Gehl Archi-
tects, n.d.). As the project partner, Gehl Architects presented the challenge of extending play beyond the 
playground.
Gehl Architects is an urban research and -design company offering expertise and consultancy in city plan-
ning, landscape architecture, and urban design with a people-focused approach (Gehl Architects, 2019). 
The company is committed to increasing people’s quality of life through the design of the urban environ-
ment. The co-founder of the company, Jan Gehl, is an advocate for the pacification of trafficked urban spa-
ces and their transformation into pedestrian environments (Gehl, 1996). This has had a significant influence 
on the perspective of the project and how it has been approached. 
Gehl Architects has adapted the tools used to collect data on public life to better understand how the 
==PAGE:11==
1.3 Problem Statement 
The problem statement has been formulated based on the research and knowledge collected throughout 
the project. It has gone through multiple iterations as more insights were gathered and the underlying 
problems were revealed. The final problem statement being: The urban environment is not designed 
for children to play on their own terms.
The change in play culture means that play is currently being controlled by multiple factors, such as city- 
planning and development and parental involvement and as a consequence, it ends up being restricted 
to playgrounds.
Play is an essential part of children’s development, but it is not seen as competency-building, why it is not 
being prioritized by different stakeholders (Churchman, 2003). This is despite play being directly linked to 
the progression of motor, cognitive, and social development in children. 
city is experienced through the lens of children from the age of zero to five (Bernard van Leer Foundation, 
2018). Gehl has been a part of several projects and initiatives that focus on the children-centered expe-
rience in urban planning, which has generated a new understanding and inspired cities to turn public 
spaces into more engaging PLAYces, whether it being a sidewalk, a bus stop, or a pocket park. This has led 
to initiatives such as painted street games, interactive hangout installations, and painted staircases (Gehl 
Architects, n.d.).

1.5 Definitions
This section briefly defines frequently used terms throughout the report. It is important that the reader 
become familiar with the definitions to have the best conditions for understanding the report, as the terms 
may vary in other contexts. The definitions are based on the discovery made in the research of the project, 
otherwise, the source is cited.
03
Integrate play into the urban environment of Copenhagen
1.4 Research Question
The research question has been developed to address the concerns outlined in the problem statement. 
Based on the knowledge gained throughout the project, the initial challenge posed was: How to imple-
menent play into the city outside playgrounds. This thesis, focusing on design and concept development, 
addresses the following question:
	
How can we encourage momentarily playful interactions for children between the age 	 	
	
of seven to ten on the curbside in the urban environment of Copenhagen?
This includes the following underlying question to research: 
•	How do we encourage children to play for 5-10 minutes on their daily commute?
•	How can we create a solution that accommodates others outside the target group?
•	How can we implement the solution homogeneously into the urban space?
•	
Play: Play is an engagement in an activity for enjoyment and recreation initiated by the child. 
The motivation for engagement is intrinsic behavior without an intention of practical purpose. 
•	
Momentarily play: Momentarily play is a shorter play experience that lasts between five and ten 
minutes. 
•	
Playground: A playground is a delimited area designed to provide children with a safe environ-
ment for physical play. The play area and the equipment, such as swingset, slide, etc., may vary 
from the different playgrounds. The playground does not cover other types of play spaces, 
such as sports facilities or non-detective play areas where play occurs, e.g. Den Sorte Plads.  
•	
Game principles: Game principles comprise of the engagement and challenges set and required 
of the player. The principles are expressed differently depending on the learning environments, 
including strategies, memory, sequence recognition, repetition, problem-solving, reacting to 
stimuli, etc.  
•	
Copenhagen: Copenhagen is delimited to the metropolitan area. This includes the city center, 
and the boroughs; Nørrebro, Østerbro, and Vesterbro. 
 
==PAGE:13==
Introduction
04
•	
Curbside and -zone: Curbside is the side of a street limited to pedestrians. The curbside can have 
different sizes and appearances through various pavement materials. The curbside zone is the 
area where the buildings meet the city (København Kommune, 2018). 
•	
Community: A community is a social group living in a common territory having one or more 
additional common ties. With the common territory being a geographically defined neigh-
borhood, and common ties being social relationships (Hillery, 1982). The feeling of community 
is the subjective measure of positive feelings that inhabitants have for each other and their 
community. 
•	
Children: Children are defined as human beings under the age of 18 years old. However, the 
main focus of this report is the average, able-bodied children between the ages of 7-10 living 
in the metropolitan area of Copenhagen. 
•	
Pedagogue:  A pedagogue is a person who is educated to take care of and support children’s 
formation and development in different age groups in their daily appearance in various institu-
tions. The word is used as the direct translation of the danish pædagogue, instead of the English 
equivalent educator.

02
 LITERATURE STUDY
==PAGE:15==
02
Literature Study
The aim of this chapter is to provide an understanding of play and its effect on children’s development. 
Only by understanding play, can we begin to understand how to shape better city environments for chil-
dren. Several design principles and approaches have been examined to apply relevant terminology and 
methods to the design process. 
Integrate play into the urban environment of Copenhagen
2.1 Play
From early childhood, children are seen engaging in play regardless of their gender, nationality, culture, or 
economic status (Unicef, 2018), which makes play universal. The United Nations Convention on the Rights 
of the Child (UN CRC) acknowledges play as a fundamental human right. It is explicitly stated in article 31 
(UN Committee, 2013): 
	
“State Parties recognize the right of the child to rest and leisure, to engage in play and recreational                  	
	
activities appropriate to the age of the child and to participate freely in cultural life and the arts.” 
This statement is backed up by the president of the National Institute for Play, Dr. Stuart Brown, who states, 
”play is as fundamental as any other aspect of life, including sleep and dreams” (Henig, 2008).
In the investigation of the literature, it became clear that experts from different fields of study have sepa-
rate definitions of play, making it difficult to define. This is primarily because the definitions are based on 
subjective or emotional criteria such as ’fun’, which can be difficult to apply in theory, as it is based on pre-
sumption, which makes it a non-adaptive value. However, it has been possible to identify a general agree-
ment on the key characteristics of play based on Lester and Russell’s (2010) examination, which is build on 
contemporary research. First, play brings feelings of enjoyment, motivation, and pleasure. Secondly, the 
act of play is done without the intention of a valuable output but rather for the joy and entertainment of 
the action. Lastly, play is more than enjoyment, as it is essential to children’s well-being and development 
(Lester & Russell, 2010; Burghardt, 2005).
Furthermore, UN Committee (2013) defines play as “any behaviour, activity or process initiated, controlled 
and structured by children themselves.”  This is, however, not equal to adults not engaging in play. Actually, 
adults also benefit from the positive impact play brings (Bentway, 2021).
Definition of Play
Based on the research and literature on the topic, we define play in this report as engagement in an 
activity for enjoyment and recreation initiated by the child. The motivation for engagement is intrinsic 
behaviour without an intention of practical purpose. Play is a fundamental and important aspect of 
childhood enjoyment and a crucial part of children’s development.
06
==PAGE:16==
Vertigo play:
Vertigo play is the perception that you 
or your surroundings are spinning or 
moving (Merriam-Webster, n.d.). Ver-
tigo play gives a thrill and can create 
an adrenaline rush from the body’s perception 
of potential danger. It is a sensation that can be 
obtained through physical, rough-and-tumble 
play, with the challenge of maintaining equi-
librium. Examples of vertigo play throughout a li-
fetime are being thrown in the air as a baby, being 
swung around, or climbing a tree.
Challenge play:
A challenge is stimulating a task or 
problem, a thing to overcome, which 
suggests the result of improvement 
of oneself after a struggle (Merri-
am-Webster, n.d.). This type of play often in-
volves rules, with the goal being overcoming 
the challenge rather than winning or losing. 
Examples of challenge play are participating in a 
football match or playing a game of solitaire with 
cards.
Construction play:
Constructing is the act of combining, 
arranging, or using various elements 
to create something different (Merri-
am-Webster, n.d.). This type of play 
is considered to be a creative form of play that 
often involves objects. Examples of construction 
play are building a tower out of bricks or using pen 
and paper to create a drawing.
Simulation play:
Simulation can be described as the 
act of pretend or imitation (Merri-
am-Webster, n.d.). Simulation play 
involves socio-dramatic, symbolic, or 
imaginative play. It is often seen with a child 
pretending to be a different character in an ima-
ginative situation. An example is children playing 
house, where they imagine themselves as parents, 
babies, dogs, etc.
2.1.1 Type of Play
Play manifests itself in many ways (Lester & Russell, 2010), with various definitions and dissections of nume-
rous types of play. One of the more common definitions is by Bob Hughes (1996), who divides play into 
15 categories (Appendix 01.3). This division is used, among others, by professional organizations such as 
The Children’s Play Council (CPC) to understand the benefits children obtain from different types of play 
(Children’s Play Council, 2000). 
It can be difficult to identify one type of play from another, as children do not distinguish between them 
(Loudoun, Boyle, & Lund, 2022). A child can build with bricks, constructive play, while pretending it is an 
imagined city they are part of, simulation play, creating a smooth transition between the different types. 
Therefore, in this project, the different types of play are perceived as a broader spectrum of activities that 
can be combined crosswise. The spectrum is divided into four main types of play based on Bonham’s disse-
ction (Bonham, 2014), which embrace multiple different play styles. The activities range from highly active 
physical play to static fantasizing. The four types of play are; vertigo-, challenge-, simulation- and construc-
tion play. 
07
Integrate play into the urban environment of Copenhagen
Figure 1 - Play spectrum.
==PAGE:17==
Literature Study
The insufficient amount of time children spend playing freely is cited by the UN CRC as of the most concer-
ning emerging trends relating to play (UN Committee, 2013). The societal tendency to focus on educational 
demands and organized competitive recreation, activities imposed by adults, results in limiting the ability 
of many children to realize the rights defined in article 31. The imposed activities reduce the time available 
to self-directed activities and sometimes force or pressure the children into participation, not of their own 
choosing. This is concerning, given that the absence of time in which children are free of demands and 
activities that are not determined or controlled by adults can be damaging to their well-being and devel-
opment (UN Committee, 2013). Furthermore, the children’s ability to engage in play becomes limited and 
negatively affected when structured by adults (Skår & Krogh, 2009). 
Play being universal is demonstrated by the fact that play patterns are similar across cultures and nations, 
despite possible disparities in play styles, rules, and equipment used (Castella, 2018). The current generati-
on of children engages in the same kinds of play as the ones prior, but the play is usually disguised in new 
styles (Appendix 03.7). LEGO (2018), discovered through a cross-cultural survey that the current generati-
on’s preference for play differed from their parents. Where the parents preferred physical play, the current 
generation had a preference for digital play. This difference might be a symptom of the general societal 
development and technological advancement between the generations (Lego, 2018). Thus it can be argu-
ed, that play has been significantly impacted by digital development. However, physical play in the role of 
sports activities was still ranked as the second most preferred type of play among the current generation 
of children (Lego, 2018). 
Figure 2 - Children’s preferred play activities Denmark (Lego, 2018).

all forms of screen time including 
watching the TV, films or YouTube

computer games or taking pictures.
==PAGE:18==
Motor abilities:
Motor skills are the function that uses muscle movements to carry out specified activities. These 
activities involve being able to bike, run or walk. Motor skills are achieved by cooperating with the 
body’s nervous system, muscles, and brain. The ages of three to five are considered critical for chil-
dren’s development of motor skills (Castella, 2018; Tilley, Anning & Welles, 2002). There are two types 
of major motor skills; gross and fine motor skills. 
Gross motor skills require the use of large muscle groups, and these allow for the performance of 
activities such as walking, balancing, and crawling. These are the first of the two to be developed 
in the child. Fine motor skills are the use of smaller muscle groups, such as fingers and toes. They 
are developed to perform tasks that require more precision, such as tying a shoelace or brushing 
one’s teeth (Stallings, 1973).
2.2 Child Development
Child development refers to the sequence of physical, cognitive, and emotional changes that happens 
in a child from birth until the conclusion of adolescence. Childhood can be divided into three stages of 
life; early childhood, middle childhood, and late childhood (National Research Council, 1984). Each of the 
phases has a different degree of development in motor, cognitive, and social-emotional development. The 
age span of the phases varies throughout different studies as they are based on the children’s level of skill 
development.
Middle childhood spans the ages 6-12 and is universally marked as a distinct period between major devel-
opmental transition points (National Research Council, 1984). Among other reasons, for the development 
of the child’s cognitive and social-emotional skills, as they encounter a new educational system and social 
connections (Castella, 2018).
Sigmund Freud described this stage as the latency stage and argued that sexual impulses are repressed 
during this stage. He believed that the repressed sexual energy instead is channeled into industrious, 
asexual activities such as learning, hobbies, and social relationships. Consequently, making this the stage 
where healthy social- and communication skills are developed (Mannoni, 2015). Jean Piaget’s cognitive 
development theory also argues for the importance of this period, given that the child begins to develop 
a more logical and systematic understanding of the world (Wadsworth, 1996).
The child’s skills are divided into the three following categories; motor-, cognitive and social-emotional 
skills.
09
Integrate play into the urban environment of Copenhagen
==PAGE:19==
Literature Study
Cognitive abilities:
Studies have found evidence suggesting that play helps foster and build cognitive processes and 
assist learning. Researchers claim that play allows children to interact with the environment to 
construct their own knowledge of the world and adapt to it by creating new learning experiences 
(Gleave & Hamilton, 2012).  
Through play, children learn how to handle difficult situations and recover from or adapt to them, 
which helps them to develop strategies for reacting to the equivalent real situations (Lester & Rus-
sell, 2008). Play can therefore help to create the framework for children to better prepare them for 
the real world. Because of the difficulty of measuring cognitive learning and therefore the cogniti-
ve influence that play can have, the studies on the subject are complex and not entirely consistent.
Social-Emotional abilities:
Social-emotional abilities are fostered throughout childhood and encompass the child’s capacity 
for understanding, experiencing, and managing their emotions, which then enables them to form 
relationships with others. A study done by Gleave and Hamilton (2012) found that the primary 
motive of children’s engagement in physical play is for social and enjoyment reasons. Play has the 
power to energize a wide audience, prompt connections, and foster relationships between people 
of different ages and abilities (Bentway, 2021). 
Additionally, play can remove cultural barriers and other boundaries helping children understand 
others who differs from themselves (Gleave & Hamilton, 2012). Friendships are important for sup-
porting mental health, social- and psychological development in children (Guroglu et al., 2008). 
Children who are able to play freely with their peers, learn about social dynamics and develop so-
cial skills that help them with cooperating, negotiating, sharing, and resolving conflicts (Ginsburg, 
2007).

Literature Study

==PAGE:22==
2.2.1 Play Deprivation in Child Development
Considering the connection between play and the child’s development, it is crucial to understand the high-
cost consequences of neglecting play throughout childhood (Pellis & Pellis, 2006; Siviy et al., 2003; Bateson, 
2005). The importance of play is present in how children’s overall physical and/or mental well-being is eva-
luated, where the absence of play is used as a diagnostic indicator, with a lack of interest in playing consi-
dered a great concern (Loizillon et al., 2017). This is a contradiction to the general association of play being 
relegated as non-essential or an elective luxury (Brown, 2014).
Through multiple studies, Brown (2014) found that the deprivation of play, especially during the first 10 years 
of childhood, was linked to major emotional dysregulations, such as depression, a tendency for non-adap-
tability, diminished impulse control, less self-regulation, diminished aggression management, shallowness, 
and fragility in relation to other people. This was compared to subjects with play-sufficient backgrounds, 
who tended to have better strategies and adaptability for managing stressful situations (Brown, 2014). 
In experiments carried out on rats, the rat pups that were subjected to deprivation of rough play had dif-
ficulties in becoming functional adult rats (Pellis & Pellis 2013). The comparison between human and rat 
development through play is too far to bridge scientifically. However, as Brown (2014) also mentions, the 
connection between the social inability to differentiate appropriate from inappropriate aggression, is fasci-
nating.  
Integrate play into the urban environment of Copenhagen
13
2.3 Designing Cities for People
Designing cities for people is a human-oriented approach that prioritizes the needs of the citizens throug-
hout the planning and development, rather than concentrating on the buildings and the traffic (Frisk, 2013). 
Planning and developing from the perspective of the everyday citizen, considering the life and the space in 
between the buildings, enables the ability to provide the best physical framework for a livable environment 
(Frisk, 2013).
Historically temples and monuments were designed to impress, suggesting that the drive for the urban 
environment to favor the public is not entirely new (Bonham, 2014). One of the modern pioneers in desig-
ning cities for people is the architect and urban planner, Jan Gehl, and critic of suburban development 
Jane Jacobs. Both emphasize that the design of the cities impacts citizens’ actions (Bonham, 2014), why it is 
important to develop cities on a human scale. Gehl (2010) argues that the issues of gathering or dispersing 
people can be perceived in a broader planning context from a larger scale; the city and regional plans, on 
a medium scale; the neighborhood and urban plans, and on a small scale; where people move. Especially, 
the small scale is interesting, with the importance the sidewalks have in a neighborhood in relation to the 
safety, contact, and assimilation of children. Sidewalks, where people move, sit and play, are all involved in 
creating a viable and safe neighborhood (Jacobs, 1961). 
	
”A good city is like a good party – you stay for longer than you plan” (Gehl, 2010).
One of the architectural theories that support the development of attractive urban space for people, is
==PAGE:23==
Literature Study
14
12 Urban Quality Criteria (Appendix 01.1), which originates from 3 central values: Protection, Comfort, and 
Enjoyment identified by Gehl (2010). The criterion, Opportunities for Play and Exercise, which emphasizes 
”physical activity, exercise, play, and street entertainment by day and night in summer and winter”, is particularly 
relevant for this project.
The general activities in the city fall into three main categories; necessary-, optional-, and social activities, 
see figure 4 (Gehl, 2010). The necessary activities are the movement from A to B in everyday life, for instance, 
to and from school, the grocery shop, etc., in a direct route. Optional activities occur when there is a desire 
to participate, and a time and a place favorable to participating, e.g. sitting on a bench to rest or observe 
others. Social activities emerge in interaction and conversations with others, e.g. children playing and pe-
ople meeting for socializing (Gehl, 2010). 
Optional- and social activities are conditioned by the physical setting of the space (Gehl, 1996). Bonham 
(2014) further relates the aesthetics of the urban space to the amount of optional- and social activities ta-
king place. 
Figure 4 - Three types of activities (Gehl 2010).
By creating the conditions for optional- and social activities to occur, the probability of a more livable en-
vironment will be greater, as these types of activities often result in an extended stay (Gehl, 2010). The urban 
environment should be designed for the speed of walking rather than at the speed of riding vehicles (Gehl, 
2010). When people walk, it is uncomplicated to stop, change direction, manoeuvre, or switch to different 
activities, such as standing, sitting, running, or playing, which benefits life in the city (Gehl, 2010). The urban 
environment becomes more interesting with the lingering of people, as it leads to the attraction of busines-
ses and activities in the streets, which in turn attracts more people, creating a virtuous cycle (Bonham, 2014).
==PAGE:24==
Integrate play into the urban environment of Copenhagen
15
2.3.1 Play in the City
Everyday life in public spaces offers opportunities for numerous different types of activities. This is impor-
tant, especially for children, as the opportunity for play and recreation is essential (UN Committee, 2013). In 
the planning and development of urban spaces, children’s needs are often ignored in broad development 
policies, strategies, and practices, as they do not recognize the significance of play for child development 
(Churchman, 2003). Furthermore, the quality of the environment influences children’s ability to play (Lester 
& Russell, 2010).
The increase in the commercialization of public spaces has resulted in children becoming more excluded 
(UN Committee, 2013). Children’s play has become increasingly limited to playgrounds, compared to earlier 
generations recalling the streets and sidewalks as the locations where most of their play occurred (Karsten, 
2006). Playgrounds function as a great meeting spot for children, initiating other social activities. However, 
the playgrounds have limited use, as the spaces are unsuccessful in meeting children’s needs or expectati-
ons, and children between the age of 8-13 prefer to spend their time in a variety of other locations (Brock-
man, Fox, & Russell, 2011).
Play in the city is about reconsidering what and for whom the public spaces are created, and how the spaces 
can be transformed to inspire and invite to play (Day, 2017). Besides the constructed play areas, such as the 
playgrounds and ball courts, the city can be the host for more spontaneous, playful activities by re-purpo-
sing the urban environment. 
Generally, there is a decreasing tolerance for children in public spaces (UN Committee, 2013). The opinion is 
that children disturb the public order, but according to Blinkert (2004), it is rather the opposite, the public 
order often disturbs children. In the urban setting, traffic becomes more prioritized and occupies more of 
the scarce space, leading to a decline in children’s outdoor play and causing a reduction in independent 
mobility (Atkin, Gorely, Biddle, Marshall, & Cameron, 2008). Greater independence to play outside, without 
adult supervision, has been linked to higher levels of physical activity in children (Brockman, Fox, & Russell, 
2011). When play is predominately structured by adults, the children’s activity level decreases and their crea-
tive expression becomes limited and affected (Skår & Krogh, 2009). The caregivers’ safety concerns restrict 
the space the children have and push the play activities into playgrounds (Carver, Timperio & Crawford 2008; 
Gehl, 2010). 
The playground is built to create a safe space for the children to play. However, it also means that the chil-
dren are not included in the urban space and end up being ”hidden away”. This increases the amount of time 
children spend in spaces designed and controlled by adults (Spencer & Blades, 2006). Particularly in public 
life, where the adult is responsible for the design and development of spaces for play, typically without al-
lowing the children the opportunity to express their wants and their needs (Churchman, 2003).
==PAGE:25==
Children’s play belongs to children, and play areas should not be destroyed by adults’ agendas or insensitive 
planning and creations that segregate children and restrict and control their play (Lester & Russell, 2010). This 
segregation and exclusion of children in urban development has significant implications for their develop-
ment as citizens (UN Committee, 2013). This impact the strength of civil society as the children are not con-
sidered citizens with rights. Therefore, it is essential that development policies and planners acknowledge 
children as rights holders to a greater extent when cities are developed.
2.3.2 Seasons in the City
As Copenhagen experiences all four seasons, it is important to address the various factors that come with 
a change in weather. The winter season, with weather like snow, can help break down the barriers of playg-
rounds (Brockman, Fox, & Russell, 2011). Especially, the snow blurs the line between the children’s and adults’ 
places and changes the visual expression of the playground (Gudova, 2016). Play equipment that previously 
had a specific function, such as swings, can have other functions as the children’s imagination is set wild. An 
issue with removing the physical barriers around play areas is that it can be difficult for children to determi-
ne whether the space is safe/unsafe or appropriate/inappropriate for play (Gudova, 2016).
It is also important to note, that when it is rainy, windy, or cold, citizens tend to spend less time in public spa-
ces and move more quickly - on average, with a speedup of 1.5 km/h - and reserved primarily for necessary 
tasks (Gehl, 2010).
Literature Study
16
2.3.3 Inclusive City
Inclusive design principles are fundamental in designing cities that aim to embrace anyone. The child’s 
independent movement is limited in the city, due to inadequate physical abilities such as height, strength, 
balance, flexibility, movement speed, etc. (Clarkson, Coleman, Keates & Lebbon, 2003). The children do not fit 
into the margins of what the city is designed for, which limits their use of the city. As they develop, they get 
a better view, and their other physical abilities improve, giving them the possibility to make better use of 
the city (Gehl, 2010).
The inclusive design principles help design and develop environments which everyone can access. Whether 
it is children of every age to the elderly with poor physical capability. The key aspect of inclusive design is to 
expand the target group, including as many users as possible, without compromising the user experience. 
The needs of that target group should be adequately met. However, the ability of the product or service 
to meet the needs and demands of others outside the target group may be compromised if the design is 
tailored too closely to the requirements of the specified group (Clarkson, Coleman, Keates, & Lebbon, 2003).
==PAGE:26==
Integrate play into the urban environment of Copenhagen
17
Equitable Use: Useful to individuals with diverse abilities. The play experience and features must 
be accessible in several ways, and the physical effort should be minimized. 
Flexibility in Use: Accommodates a variety of individual preferences and abilities. Play experien-
ces should include adaptable features to accommodate users of different dimensions, abilities, 
and paces. 
Simple and Intuitive Use: Easy to understand, regardless of individuals’ experience, knowledge, 
language skills, or current concentration level, e.g. simple signs that are easy for most to interpret.
Perceptible Information: Communicate necessary information effectively to the user, regardless 
of the user’s sensory abilities, e.g. extruding symbols to help those with visual impairment.
Tolerance for Error: Reduce risks and negative consequences of accidental or unintended actions. 
The child should be able to use the play equipment and features in unintended ways without 
compromising the experience.
Low Physical Effort: The product should provide a comfortable and effective experience, where 
the user experience as little fatigue as possible while using the product. The entrance to the play 
experience needs to be a low physical effort to create the same experience for all children.
Size and Space for Approach and Use: The product should be appropriately sized and spaced for 
approach, reach, manipulation, and use, regardless of the user’s body size, posture, or mobility. 
2.3.4 Universal Design
The Universal Design (UD) principles are ideal when designing for inclusivity, as they advocate design that 
recognizes and acknowledges the demands of a wide variety of users (Clarkson, Coleman, Keates, & Lebbon, 
2003). To apply UD principles successfully, one is required to have an understanding of how abilities vary 
with age, disability, environment, or circumstances (Story, Mueller, & Mace, 1998). The principles support in-
dependence, as they aim to engage play opportunities in a barrier-free environment 
The seven principles of UD, including examples for play spaces, are as follows (Story, Mueller, & Mace, 1998; 
The Center for Universal Design, 1997);

2.3.5 Design for the Senses
Developing a design that can activate multiple senses creates an experience that embraces people who 
might be limited in their sensory reception, thus making it more inclusive. Furthermore, designing for dif-
ferent senses can support the individual’s abilities to receive information, aiding them in exploring and 
learning about their surroundings.
Our childhoods are spent trying to understand and learn to navigate the world by using our senses (Castella, 
2018). Children receive information through the use of their senses by touching, seeing, moving, hearing, 
etc., helping develop motor-, cognitive-, and social skills, such as language development, social interaction, 
etc. (Castella, 2018). The information received physically through our senses is processed into an understan-
ding by our cognition, which then shapes the basis of our reality (Castella, 2018). 
In an interview with neurobiologist, Schilhab, it was mentioned, that we associate and connect objects with 
more than their abstract meaning. For instance, a banana is associated with the visualization, taste, smell, 
and texture of it or can even be connected to a situation. This supports that interactions with artifacts are 
experiences that are sensory-based (Beckett, 2019). Therefore, it is essential to consider how the senses are 
affected in the interaction, and how the influence of the senses can support a child’s development. In the 
following section, some of the common senses used to explore the city are discussed, they are based on the 
descriptions provided by Castella (2018).
Literature Study
18
Visual - the sense of seeing. 
The visual sense is the sense of seeing. Design for sight includes factors related to seeing color, 
visual composition, light, and form. Sight is a sense that develops and changes throug hout life. 
As children grow, they will use their visual senses to look for patterns that can help their cognitive 
schemes, skills, and memory. The monocular sight is divided into the central vision, which is used for loo-
king at details, and the peripheral vision, which creates an understanding of the context of what is being 
seen. There are, in particular, two factors that are interesting when looking at designing for the vision. The 
first is the ability to capture the attention of the sight by using movement. The second is how colors can 
have an influence on our perceptions, decisions, and behaviors.
Auditory - the sense of hearing. 
The ears are constantly taking in information about our surroundings and how we should act 
accordingly. This information is collected automatically and then processed in the cognition, 
creating an understanding of where the sound is coming from and what it means. In the audi-
tory sense, there is a distinction between hearing and listening. Whereas hearing is a constant, 
listening takes practice and is important for the child’s development, as listening is used to receive infor-
mation from others and keep safe. Listening is a vital skill for learning when the child is not yet able to read. 
Important factors of the auditory sense are, firstly, the ability to grab the attention by using loud noises and 
secondly, that people can become habituated to sounds, meaning that after a while, they will completely 
ignore certain sounds.  
==PAGE:28==
Olfactory - the sense of smell.
The sense is automatically gathering information to be processed, like the sense of hearing. This 
sense is closely connected to parts of the brain that register memories, why it can evoke feelings 
of comfort, excitement, and fright. Because of the connection between the center for memory 
and the sense of smell is often used by companies to evoke memories, emotions, and associations with a 
brand or product. 
Vestibular - the sense of movement, balance, and spatial orientation.
The sense prevents humans from falling over when walking or standing still. It is developed in 
early childhood, where it is in a rough shape and is refined with the child’s development. This is 
seen by children learning to, e.g. balance and walk. 
Proprioception - the sense of bodily awareness. 
Proprioception is the sense of bodily awareness. It refers to the knowledge of the positioning and move-
ment of limbs and muscles (Tuthill & Azim, 2018). The sense of proprioception is present in every muscle mo-
vement, making it possible to walk without consciously thinking about it, touch the elbow with eyes closed, 
or steer a fork into the mouth whilst looking at a phone. The refinement of this sense is developed along 
with gross and fine motor skills and allows children to interact with the physical world.
Whether there are five, seven, or twenty-one senses is a subject of debate in the literature. The first menti-
on of the five senses is credited to Aristotle in his work De Anima (Aristotle, 350 BC), and are; sight, hearing, 
smell, taste, and touch. In recent times, more senses have been added, contributed by multiple studies, in 
particular, vestibular and proprioception. Six of seven senses have been discussed above; the seventh, the 
sense of taste, has been left out given its unimportance to the project.
2.3.6 Human-Centered Design
Designing products, services, and systems requires a fundamental understanding of the human perspecti-
ve. The Human-Centered Design (HCD) approach puts human needs, capabilities, and behavior first, then 
aims to design to accommodate those, leading to more usable solutions (Norman, 2013).
The principles of HCD apply even if the HCD process is not followed. The designer must design with not
Integrate play into the urban environment of Copenhagen
19
Tactile - the sense of touch. 
The sense of touch is stimulated when the skin has contact. There are receptors all over the body, 
with areas such as fingers, lips, and feet having the most. The four types of receptors are the 
sensing of vibration, slippage, the stretching of the skin, and sensing textures. It is one of the first 
physical senses that is developed. Children use it to explore and learn by touching materials with different 
textures and temperatures. There is a special system for the social and emotional touch, which is developed 
throughout childhood, this touch system is essential for building healthy relationships, for example giving 
hugs to feel safe and loved. When designing for the tactile sense, it is important to think about the material 
temperature, surface, form, and texture and how the body is able to interact with it.
==PAGE:29==
2.3.7 User-Centered Design
A methodology that is closely related to HDC principles is User-Centered Design (USD) principles. Both prin-
ciples make use of a similar approach and are used interchangeably in the literature. The UCD approach 
concentrated on the specific target group and the development of a solution that fulfills the user’s need 
through ongoing user involvement  and evaluation (Gould & Lewis, 1985).
Generally, UCD follows three principles, which are defined as follows (Gould & Lewis, 1985): 
1. Early focus on users and tasks: implies an understanding of who the users will be.
2. Empirical measurement: intended user should use simulations and prototypes in actual usage scenarios, 
and their performance and reactions should be observed, recorded, and analyzed. 
3. Iterative design proceeding: the problems that would be found through user testing must be fixed, crea-
ting a cycle of design, test, measure, and redesign, repeated as often as necessary.
Literature Study
Designing for children
Designing for children differs from designing for the general adult. Some of the unique factors that make 
designing for children different are listed in the box beneath (Castella, 2018). It is important to accommodate 
these factors, and the developmental level of the child, to prevent them from exclusion.

themselves verbally.

Children experience rapid development both physically, cognitively, socially, and emotional-

•

tion, and response. 
•	
Children enjoy challenges because it can lead to feelings of accomplishment or success from 
trying.
•	
Children enjoy repetition.
•	
Children are less forgiving and patient and will easily get frustrated compared to adult users.
•	
Children appreciate feedback through interaction.
•	
There are higher safety standards for children’s toys and equipment comparatively.
•	
Children’s physical dimensions vary greatly and are very different from the average adult’s
only the end-user in mind but with users as a system in which everything is interconnected (Norman, 2018). 
The HCD process begins with understanding people and the needs the design intends to meet. Observati-
on is a great tool for providing this understanding, as people themselves often are unaware of their actual 
needs (Norman, 2013). Additionally, the HCD principles are about finding the real problem rather than the 
symptom of the problem. If the fundamental problem is solved, the symptom will disappear (Norman, 2018).

2.3.8 Behavioral Design
To change behaviors, in our case, children’s interactions with their surroundings in the urban environment, 
it is essential to understand the underlying factors that drive human behavior for the sake of designing for 
purposeful change. As the project aims to change the current behaviors identified in the city, it is exami-
ned how frameworks, principles, and tools, within behavioral design, can be used to design for a desired 
behavior change. Behavioral design pursues to actively change problematic behaviors through purposeful 
interventions (Cash et al., 2017) or as described by Khadikar et al. (2020):
”Behavioural design has the goal to explicitly and ethically realise positive behaviour, desired by both the indi-
vidual and society; the object of design is behaviour itself, which is explicitly understood and designed for using 
behavioural theories and brought into effect by actively changing user psychology with the help of artifacts.” 
Meaning that certain behaviors, that are desired both by the individual and the society at large, can be 
obtained through the use of inspiration, nudging, persuasion, or motivation in artifacts (Tromp, Hekkert, & 
Veerbeek, 2011). To be able to understand and describe human behaviors, the following two are being in-
vestigated the behavior (Antecedent-Behavior-Consequence, ABC) and the cognition (Dual process theory).
Behavior
To understand certain behavioral interactions and understand where and when they can be influenced, Mil-
tenberger’s (2011) ABC model can be used. Applying the model allows for an understanding of behaviors as 
actions, with the antecedent being the trigger of the behavior and the consequence being a result thereof. 
According to the model, it is possible to change the behavior in all three steps using different strategies. 
Antecedent strategies rely on interventions prior to the unwanted behavior, motivating it in a desirable 
direction. Consequence strategies rely on interventions after the performance of the undesired behavior 
(Abrahamsen et al., 2005). The breakdown of the model allows the designer to describe the behavioral inte-
ractions and explain when and where the behavior can be influenced. Examples of interventions could be 
rewards, fees, and feedback. It is important to note that consequence strategies primarily rely on the indivi-
dual’s own self-reflection on their actions.
Cognition
To understand how thoughts and behaviors are linked, the dual process theory (Vaisey, 2009) states that 
thoughts arise as a result of two different processes. The two processes are implicit, automatic responses 
also described as an unconscious process, and explicit, controlled responses, recognized as a conscious 
process.
Given that the implicit system is automatic, it guarantees quick decision-making. The system builds on asso-
ciative responses to situations and builds on intuition. The explicit system is controlled and is also referred 
to as the rational mind. It requires working memory, thus making the decision-making processes compara-
tively slower and more deliberate. The system builds on introspection and explicit reasoning. According to 
Kahneman (2011), design interventions can target the two systems, with either conscious or 
==PAGE:31==
unconscious interventions. The two systems help explain why it might be challenging to alter human be-
havior because it rarely is enough to target the explicit system. To achieve an understanding of how intera-
ctions can lead to changes in human behavior, Cash et al. (2017) developed a model that combines Vaisey’s 
dual process theory and Miltenberger’s ABC model. The model shows how the path of the interaction th-
rough the behaviors has an influence on which of the two cognitive systems is affected.
Figure 5 - Combination of the ABC model and the Dual Process Theory as proposed by Cash et. al (2017).
Literature Study
22
The paths leading from the artifact to the person indicate a strategy coupled with a behavior and a cogniti-
on. Depending on the type of artifact, or the kind of exerting influence used, the strategy can be modified 
for the intended outcome. When designing for behavioral change, there are unforeseen elements that can 
affect the intended outcome of the strategy, this includes culture and context. 
The strategies can be considered either structural or informational. Where the structural aim to change con-
textual factors, such as limiting access, informational strategies are more focused on changing knowledge, 
awareness, norms, and attitude (Cash, et al. 2017). Though it might be very efficient, structural intervention 
strategies often aim at removing choice, which is deemed highly unethical.

Methodology
This chapter describes the overall methodology of this project’s design process. The limitations of the cho-
sen approach and methods will be discussed continuously as these are reviewed. 
3.1 Double Diamond
The project has followed the Double Diamond design process. The process is divided into two diamonds, 
the first focusing on the problem space and the second on the solution space. The first diamond aided 
in understanding and uncovering the root of the problem, with the following diamond consisting of the 
development of a solution to solve it. Each diamond consists of two phases, a divergent and a convergent. 
The Double Diamond framework is beneficial to a design project, as it works similarly to a map through 
the development from problem to solution. It consists of four phases; Discover, Define, Develop, and Deliver 
(Drew, 2019). The use of the framework has helped create an overview of the design process and models 
used throughout and has supported the planning, management, and execution of the project, see figure 6.
Figure 6 - The Double Diamond process of the project.

The Double Diamond is represented as a linear process, moving through the four phases from left to right. 
However, the process of this project has been iterative, going back and forth between the phases when 
new information has been discovered. Despite the visual depiction of the phases being equal, the diver-
ging phases require more time, compared to the convergent phases, with a time variation between three 
to eight weeks.
Given that the Double Diamond does not consider the logistical aspect of a design process, a Gantt Chart 
has worked as a supplement in determining the time frame of activities, along with the designation of 
clear deadlines in the completion of the four phases (Appendix 01.2). The strict deadlines have meant that 
some decisions had to be made and forced us to continue to the next phase.

==PAGE:34==
Integrate play into the urban environment of Copenhagen
The strict deadlines have meant that some decisions had to be made and forced us to continue to the 
next phase. This has impacted the outcome of each phase and, ultimately, the outcome of the project. 
Within each phase, different methods and tools have been applied. Several methods and figures have 
evolved and re-applied through several phases as the project has progressed and been iterated.
3.1.2 User Centered Design
This project has been addressed with a User Centered design approach. This is aligned with Gehl Archite-
cts working principles, ensuring an understanding of the users’ needs and experience in the public space 
(Gehl Architects, 2019). The principles of the approach have helped decrease the possible mistakes that can 
be caused by designs that do not consider the users’ needs (see section 2.3.6 and 2.3.7). 
To develop a satisfying solution, the focus has been on the user since the inception of the project. This has 
been done by selecting a specific target group, in this case, children between the ages of seven to ten. The 
inclusion of the target group has proved beneficial in the design process, especially in the development, 
testing, and evaluation of the solution. The involvement of the target group in the design process led to a 
better understanding of the children’s perception of play in the city and directly allowed them to influence 
the initial development and the final adjustment of the solution. The UCD approach involves several ite-
rations, as the users bring new information and feedback that may influence the process of the project in 
a new or different direction. The methods of UCD that have been used in the project include, but are not 
limited to; background interviews, personas, user scenarios, various types of prototyping, and user testing.
3.1.3 Universal Design Principles
With a solution that aims for integration into the public space, there is a potential for anyone to interact 
with it, hence the importance of considering designing a solution that emphasizes the UD principles (see 
section 2.3.4). The goal of the UD principle is to create usable, inclusive designs that do not discriminate 
on age, nationality, disability, etc. (Story, Mueller, & Mace, 1998). The main target group has been average, 
non-disabled children between the ages of 7 and 10. Given that the development level varies significantly 
in these years, the UD principles are very relevant, as the speed of development varies notably. The chil-
dren within the age group might not be on the same level of physical-, cognitive- or social development, 
given the explosive development of children’s physique, wit, and interpretation skills at this age span (see 
section 2.2). For instance, some children might be able to read at the age of nine, whilst some might not.
3.2 Interview
To investigate the problem area and gain deep insight and knowledge of children’s play in the city, se-
mi-structured interviews were conducted with stakeholders, including pedagogues, play experts, and city 
development architects. The interviews were used to collect firsthand personal accounts of experience, 
perceptions, and opinions (Martin & Hanington, 2012). The semi-structured approach meant that we tried 
to stick to the script of questions but allowed for detours in a conversational format. When conducting the 
interviews, we tried to remain passive, not leading the participants to an answer that might fulfill our hy-
pothesis and expectations. We decided on the semi-structured interviews to have consistency across the 
different sessions with different stakeholders. 
25
==PAGE:35==
3.3 Observations
Throughout the project, observation has been a preferred method to collect data on people’s behavior 
and movements, not only to gain preliminary insight into the Copenhagen urban environment but also 
in meeting with various proposed solutions in a setting of testing with users. All the observations can be 
found in Appendix 04. The observations were done both overtly, meaning that the subject knew they were 
being observed, and covertly, where the observer has been concealed and out of sight (Martin & Haning-
ton, 2012). 
The covert observation allowed us to unobtrusively gather data and information by observing without di-
rect participation or interference with the people or their behaviors and activities being observed (Martin 
& Hanington, 2012). To minimize the possible influence of our presence, the observations were conducted 
from a distance at a vantage point. The method is a qualitative research methodology that records the 
use of the observed public space, what people use the area, and how they use it. The observations were 
to some degree planned and structured but allowed for flexibility. As part of the Gehl methodology, we 
collected both quantitative and qualitative data from our observations. Amongst others, we measured the 
number of people within the public space, and the perceived age, we noted down their activities and their 
use of the space.
Methodology
The interviews were conducted as the first phase of uncovering the problem. To encompass different per-
spectives and get an overview of the problem, three groups of stakeholders were interviewed; pedagogu-
es, play experts, and city development architects. The full interviews can be found in Appendix 03.
The three groups of stakeholders were chosen based on their knowledge and experience on the subject of 
play in the urban environment. The pedagogues were interviewed because of their intensive knowledge 
of children and their development, as well as their first-hand experience and daily contact with our specific 
target group. The play experts were interviewed to gain an understanding of relevant factors to consider in 
the development of play equipment and an insight into methods used in the development and testing of 
play solutions. Lastly, the city development architects were interviewed to gain an understanding of their 
development strategies, how they currently target children and in the future, in addition, opportunities 
and what rules apply for integrating play into the urban space.
The interviews were conducted through various media, such as on a phone call, by zoom, and physically. 
The flexibility in the conduction method ensured the participation possibility for the interviewees. Con-
ducting the interviews through phone calls had the disadvantage of not being able to experience the 
participants’ facial expressions. The facial expressions would have allowed for the decoding of when the 
participants felt particularly excited or began to hesitate, which could have led to follow-up questions. 
However, this disadvantage was weighted against the number of participants who would not have had the 
opportunity to participate under other conditions. Additionally, some of the interviews might have been 
influenced by the personal relationship between the participant and the interviewer, given the familiarity 
between the two and the knowledge of the project. Lastly, the interviews were all conducted in Danish 
and were later translated, which creates the possibility of data getting lost in the translation.
26
==PAGE:36==
Behavioral Mapping, a place-based mapping was used to record the movement in the public space, focu-
sing on observation to identify locational patterns of behaviors (Martin & Hanington, 2012). The maps de-
pict the citizens’ engagement in various activities in a specific setting (Ng, 2016). The essential physical 
features of interest in the locations were mapped out, and the behavior recorded was categorized into 
behavioral actions, such as using play equipment and interacting with other children. Behavioral Mapping 
has the benefit of depicting people’s actions rather than their words. People may not provide an honest 
answer when asked about their daily lives and certain activities. This can be caused by several factors, such 
as; people being aware of a behavior that is against the social norm (Ng, 2016).
The initial observations took place between the 9th of September to the 1st of October 2022, with a total 
of nine rounds of observations, where children and adults’ movements were observed in selected public 
areas. These areas included paths and parks near schools. It is important to note that the observations 
were done at different times during different days in shorter periods of time. Consequently, it is not possi-
ble to see the complete picture, as the activity and behavior change based on parameters like the weather 
and the day of the week (see section 2.3.2). Details and insights from the observations were noted during 
the stakeout to ensure proper documentation.
3.4 User Involvement
With a UCD approach to the project, it has been essential to involve different stakeholders throughout the 
project. The early, continual focus and involvement of both the end-users and stakeholders have benefited 
the project with valuable insight and confirmed the requirement for a solution. The stakeholders were in-
volved through interviews and observations at the beginning of the design process and were later invited 
to a co-creation workshop, and user and usability testing of the prototypes.
 
3.4.1 Co-creation
In the development phase, the target group was invited to contribute with their knowledge, creativity, and 
opinions through a facilitated co-creation workshop. The results obtained from Co-creation be found in 
Appendix 05. The results of a workshop are heavily reliant on planning and execution. Factors such as the 
type of participants, time span, location, and resources available are all necessary to keep in mind when 
planning, due to the impact they have on the desired outcome (Mattelmäki & Visser, 2011).
The workshop was executed under the following conditions:
 
Stakeholders: 17 fourth-grade students
Location: Classroom at Skolen ved Søerne
Time: Held during a lesson
Duration: Approximately 90 minutes
27
During the workshop, we received assistance from the class’ two teachers. They helped us keep control of 
the class and rearticulate certain tasks for the children to better understand our intention. 
Integrate play into the urban environment of Copenhagen
==PAGE:37==
28
 
The articulation might have influenced the output and could have been avoided by a briefing with the tea-
cher beforehand. It would have proven beneficial for us to have had a better pedagogical understanding 
of how to deliver tasks, and of the target group’s developmental progress in understanding abstraction 
and logical thinking. As the participants were children, we were aware of selecting and creating activities 
that were not too complex and remained playful. This was done to maintain their engagement and enhan-
ce their mindset in a playful and creative matter. 
The objective of the co-creation was to encourage the participants to ideate on design ideas, as they are 
the experts on their own experiences. During the workshop, we were able to gather valuable insight into 
the children’s own opinions and the thoughts behind their ideas and creations. Given the number of chil-
dren versus two facilitators, the time available to talk about the thoughts behind their ideas was limited. 
By analyzing and interpreting the children’s ideas and creations, we had a better understanding of the 
children’s preferences and needs to be required for a final solution. Using this method ensured that the 
children had the possibility to express their needs and wishes, and proved a useful inspiration in the de-
velopment of concepts. It’s important to keep in mind that the 17 students are a small sample of a larger 
demographic, their ideas and thoughts are not considered a common opinion for the entire target group. 
The children’s background and upbringing might have had an impact on the final outcome.
3.4.2 User Testing
By testing with the users, it has been possible to gain insights into, how the end-user experience and 
interact with both our concept and prototype before the development of the final solution. It has proven 
beneficial for improving solutions both rapidly and iteratively (Martin & Hanington, 2012), as we have 
been able to gather quick feedback. Testing with the users has created a foundation for making more 
informed decisions in the design process. 
Testing with users was performed twice. The first test was an MVP test (Martin & Hanington, 2012) of the 
prototypes executed under the following conditions:
 
Stakeholders: Approximately 80 fourth to sixth-grade students
Location: Public pavement outside of Fritids- og Juniorklub Filippa
Time: After school at 12.00-15:00
Duration: 3 hours
The aim was to test which of the concepts had greater success in engagement from the children. The 
outcome of the test was limited due to the children’s hesitation to interact with the pretotypes* and their 
placement.
The second test was a usability test of the prototype executed under the following conditions:
Methodology
*A rapid experiment that aims to test ideas quickly, simply and at low scost, gathering data to validate ideas 
before building the actual prototype (Savoia, 2011)
==PAGE:38==
Integrate play into the urban environment of Copenhagen
29
The goal of the test was to create a proof of concept and test the usability of the prototype. The chil-
dren were asked to perform several tasks, that were designed to simulate and reflect the principle of the 
concept. The test was divided into two parts, a testing of the physical prototype and the game principle 
through a digital prototype. The aim of the test was to reveal possible frustrations or confusion at uni-
dentified pitfalls when interacting with the prototypes. This helped identify improvements in the design, 
thus creating a better user experience.

Exploratory User Study
This chapter presents the key stakeholders and the significant influence they had on the design process. 
The main findings, which have been collected through various methods, such as interviews, observati-
ons, and a co-creation workshop, are elaborated and reviewed with the presentation of each stakeholder. 
Integrate play into the urban environment of Copenhagen
4.1 Stakeholders
To gain a visual representation of all the stakeholders, a stakeholder map was developed (Martin & Haning-
ton, 2012). This helped with identifying the key stakeholder, the stakeholders who would be affected by the 
project. It should be noted that the stakeholder map has evolved and adjusted as the project progressed 
and will continue to evolve and expand with future work. The stakeholder map initially featured a wider 
range of stakeholders, but as the project advance and the scope narrowed, so did the relevant stakehol-
ders.
The key stakeholders of the project are identified as; children, parents, teachers/pedagogues, Copenhagen 
Municipality, landscape architects, Gehl Architects, and play experts. These stakeholders had different in-
volvement in the project, but all contributed with different methods, knowledge, and insights that helped 
the project progress through the uncovering of the different perspectives on the topic of child play in the 
cityscape.
Figure 7 - Stakeholder map. The key stakeholders are highlighted.
31
Internal stakeholders
Eksternal stakeholders
Governmental 
==PAGE:41==
Exploratory User Study
Governmental: Copenhagen Municipality
Urban designers and planners at Copenhagen Municipality are key stakeholders in the development of 
urban space in Copenhagen. Two landscape architects, Helle Hagelund and Claus Borre were interviewed. 
They work in the Park and Urban Space department of The Technical and Environmental Administration at 
Copenhagen Municipality and therefore work firsthand with developing the urban space. They both work 
with a focus on creating spaces for children in the urban environment, in particular, parks and squares. The 
full interview can be found in Appendix 03.9. 
The landscape architects provided insight and details on different initiatives and projects, mainly in parks 
and squares, that aim to accommodate children in the city. Furthermore, the two representatives acknow-
ledge that the Copenhagen Municipality does not succeed in developing a city that embraces everyone, 
despite it being the aim of their strategies.  With the strategies striving to target everyone, it ends up tar-
geting no one, as mentioned by themselves in reflection of the conversation.  
The absence of children’s play in the city is partially due to Copenhagen Municipality’s policies, agendas, 
and priorities. They recognize the children’s play outside the playgrounds as an important area, and they 
found it beneficial to incorporate it into future strategies and visions. The insights from the landscape 
architects contributed to a general understanding of the policies and strategies that currently affect the 
urban development of Copenhagen. 
The following are the main findings from the interview:
•	
In general, Copenhagen’s urban space is not designed for children, as it does not offer inventory 
suitable for children outside of playgrounds.
•	
In urban development, landscape architects are required to follow the strategies of Copenhagen 
Municipality, as they serve as a guide for what can and must be done. Providing that the strategies 
do not cover children’s play outside of the playground, it neither will be planned nor developed 
for. 
•	
The urban space of Copenhagen is limited, which leads to strong competition for the available 
space. Other areas, such as traffic, are given higher priority in the logistics of planning and de-
veloping the city compared to children and play. To address this issue, Copenhagen Municipality 
intends to design the available spaces for multipurpose use. This is also a strategy for reaching a 
diverse range of people and bringing citizens across generations together.
32
==PAGE:42==
Play experts: Kompan
Kompan is one of the manufacturers of playgrounds, that design and install public playgrounds for Copen-
hagen Municipality. Therefore, the company is an interesting stakeholder as they have extensive experi-
ence in bringing play into the city through playgrounds and play equipment. Kompan has an interest in 
developing the best play solutions for children, to support a happier community. Additionally, it is affili-
ated with Kompan Play Institute, which conducts research on children’s development and play benefits. 
Hence, their valuable knowledge on the topic. An interview with a play consultant from Kompan helped 
clarify the factors that are necessary when developing play experiences for children. The full interview 
can be found in Appendix 03.7. The factors are further elaborated in figure 8. The main findings from the 
interview are as follows; 
33
•	
Kompan identified that cross-generational play is missing from the urban space. This brought on 
the current focus on the development of play experiences outside the playgrounds, with the aim 
of gathering citizens across generations.
•	
There are two emerging trends in play, one emphasizes bringing children into nature to play with 
natural elements, and the other emphasizes increased use of technological and digital platforms.
•	
There is a significant difference in play culture and behavior between countries - even cities. Play 
on the streets has been observed to be less confined outside of the metropolitan area of Copen-
hagen. Parental anxiety is viewed as a city phenomenon in which parents are more observant of 
their children during play activities. The anxiety is believed to come from the number of residents 
in a limited space, and the traffic.

==PAGE:44==
Caregivers: parents and pedagogues
Typically, children’s play behavior is supervised and controlled by their caregivers, namely the parents. 
They have a significant impact on children’s play in the city, as the children often depend on their parents’ 
companionship and time to be escorted to and from play spaces. The parents are, therefore, considered 
interesting stakeholders. Attempts were made to interview the parents or make them participate in sur-
veys. However, they were difficult to reach, which resulted in failed attempts. Yet their behavior related 
to children’s play has been observed throughout Behavioral Mapping. This has been advantageous, as it 
provided an unbiased insight into the parents’ involvement in the children’s movement in the city. 
To gain the caregivers’ perspective on the children’s play behavior and movement in the city, four peda-
gogues were interviewed. All four pedagogues are daily involved with children in the age of the target 
group. Given their educational background in handling tasks that relate to children’s care and develop-
ment, they, in general, have a more theoretical approach to the engagement with the children, compared 
with the other caregivers. The pedagogues have contributed to a greater understanding of the target 
group’s ability, need, play behavior, and movement in the city. All interviews can be found under Appendix 
03.2-03.5.
The main findings from the four interviews are summarized as the following:
35
•	
The children are comfortable and accustomed to moving around safely in the city, as they were ra-
ised there. Additionally, the children are taught to move independently in the city by institutions. 
Fritidsklubben teaches the children to safely walk from the school to the institution as a part of the 
initial introduction. 
•	
The pedagogues assist and teach the children to become more independent individuals. At Fritids-
klubben, the children are more or less left to themselves and in charge of their own decisions. At 
this age, they generally play by themselves.
•	
Fritidsklubben uses the city’s facilities to play, e.g. Fælledparken, where the bigger area offers an op-
portunity for other types of play. The pedagogues define a clear area through an imaginative fence 
where the children are allowed to play to avoid being a nuisance to others in the public space. The 
children are reminded to be considerate of others when playing in public places, as they can take 
up a lot of it.
•	
The pedagogues had different concerns about children’s play. One of them is children not having 
enough time for independent play. Nowadays, children have longer school days and have many 
leisure activities multiple times a week. According to the pedagogues this is not necessarily bad, 
but it should not affect the children’s time for self-chosen play. This concern is shared by the UN 
CRC which emphasizes it as concerning development, that reaches globally (UN Committee, 2013).
Integrate play into the urban environment of Copenhagen
==PAGE:45==
Exploratory User Study
Children
The children, especially the target group, are crucial stakeholders, as the project aims to develop a solution 
that includes them in the urban space of Copenhagen. The solution, therefore, pursues to fulfill the chil-
dren’s needs and requirements, thus hoping to create an enjoyable experience. 
It has been crucial for the project to involve the children to gain an understanding of their behaviors and 
needs from the city. This has been accomplished by observing their patterns using Behavioral Mapping 
and receiving their input and expertise through a co-creation workshop and testing. The findings from the 
use of methods will be discussed in further detail in the following two sections.
4.2 Behavioral Mapping
Behavioral Mapping was used to record behaviors and movements systematically by observing specific 
settings in the urban environment of Copenhagen. The two settings which have been mapped are; (1) 
children’s play behavior in the urban space, and (2) children’s commute to school.
As younger children often have difficulty verbalizing their thoughts, feelings, and behaviors (Ng, 2016), 
Behavioural Maps have been a great tool to collect insight into children’s movement and play behavior in 
the chosen settings. 
The maps depict the facilities in the environ-
ment, divided into general facilities and play 
area facilities. The orange circles with numbers 
indicate the location of the facilities, with the as-
sociated actions being marked with letters, see 
figure 10. Behavioral actions are categorized into 
general and play actions, look rigth. In addition,

des of transportation to gain an understanding 
of the general flow in the urban space, and how 
it possibly disturbs children’s play behavior. 
36
Figure 9 - Behavioral actions.
==PAGE:46==
•	
The urban environment has the potential to serve as a great alternative play space (see section 
2.3.1). It was observed that children play with their environment. They balance on elevations, run 
up and down hilly terrains, and climb and jump between stone installations. 
•	
The children interact with the facilities in the urban space. Children use skate ramps, installations 
to climb on, or use as hangout spots, swings, etc. 
•	
A high number of bicycles crossed the areas for observation in a short period of time, even though 
there was no indication of cycle paths. Especially, at Enghave Plads where there was no clear path, 
which made it difficult for the others to predict the directions of the bicycles. It was observed that 
the traffic flow had a direct negative effect on the children’s play in the designated area.
•	
The caregivers were constantly surveilling and controlling the children’s movements. Especially, 
when the children approached the water foundations at Enghave Plads or the crossing path at 
Folket’s park, where bicycles likewise crossed.
To record children’s play behavior, the locations Enghave Plads and Folket’s Park were chosen as these ur-
ban spaces are located close to schools (Appendix 04.1-04.3). Furthermore, the space offers opportunities 
for structured and unstructured play. The aim of the Behavioral Map in these settings was to track the citi-
zen’s activities, with a focus on children and their interactions, see figure 10. The Behavioral Map provided a 
better understanding of the distribution of behaviors in the urban space at the given time; when children’s 
school day was over. This helped identify possible controversies and prospective actions to improve the 
use of space. The main findings identified from Behavioural Maps of ’children’s play behavior in the urban 
space’ are as follows:
