To record children’s behavior in their daily commute, locationsin proximity to schools were chosen at the 
time children were heading for school (Appendix 04.4-04.8). By observing in this setting, it was possible to 
collect a lot of data. The observations were executed in four different locations nearby schools at Nørrebro, 
see figure 11 and 12. The Behavioral Mapping provided insight into the target group’s independent move-
ment, as several of the children moved without caregivers to school, as anticipated. The main findings from 
these observations were: 
•	
The children moved on their daily commute alone, together in groups of peers, or with caregivers. 
•	
The mode of transport varied from walking, riding scooters, bicycling, skating, and carried in Cargo 
Bikes driven by guardians. Scooters, in particular, were a popular mode of transport among the 
target group.
•	
The children interacted with each other on the way to school. When the children were in the com-
pany of a caregiver, they did not interact much with other children, leading to the social interacti-
on being primarily with the caregiver. 
•	
A lot of the children who were walking alone were busy interacting with their phones.
•	
Children carried different types of play objects.
•	
In a few cases, the children used the urban environment for playful interactions. This included 
using elements that were not necessarily made for play. The children used poles as obstacle cour-
ses, raced between trees on their scooters, and balanced on the edge of flowerbeds.  
•	
Generally, the children moved carefully in the traffic and checked before crossing the roads. 
•	
The caregivers physically shielded their children with bikes to protect them from the traffic or limi-
ted their movement by holding their hands.
•	
The children’s play behavior became less constrained as they entered spaces they were accusto-
med to.

4.3 User study through Co-creation
Co-creation was used to involve the target group in the design process. The methodology and proce-
dure of the execution have been previously explained (see section 3.4.1). The co-creation workshop was 
conducted relatively early in the design process, as it was desirable to gain knowledge about the target 
group’s perception of play in the city. Play changes with time and lifestyles, but children remain experts in 
play in an evolving world (Appendix 03.7).
The co-creation was divided into two main parts; understanding the target group’s play behavior and 
ideation on solution proposals. This section focuses on the findings from the first part. The outcome of the 
second part was used for developing concepts and will be further explained later (see section 7.1).
To gather a lot of data in a short amount of time, the activity play-run was used. The objective of the acti-
vity was to gain a better understanding of the children’s play behavior. To encourage the children to write 
as many statements as possible, the activity contained an element of competition. The statements were to 
be given on the following topics; where do you play best?; what does play mean to you?; and when is play 
not fun? The data is visualized as circles, where the size is determined by the repetition of answers, and the 
colors define the category, see figure 13  and 14.

What does play mean to you?
When is play not fun?
==PAGE:52==
43
The main findings from the first part of co-creation were:
•	
The enjoyment of the play is not determined by the type of play or activities, but rather by the 
quality of the social interaction. Social interaction, especially with peers, is important for the target 
group. This is supported by Castella (2018), who claims that social development dominates at ages 
seven to eleven. The target group’s perception of the play is also heavily influenced by the type 
of interactions, with unkind social interaction being detrimental to the experience. The children 
characterize this interaction as being abandoned by peers, bad communication such as a disagre-
ement or use of swear words, violent behavior, etc. 
•	
Children’s play is not limited to a specific location, as they see the opportunity to play everywhere. 
School, including the schoolyard, Fritidsklubben, home, and friends’ houses were frequent answers 
to the preferred locations for play. None of the children stated the city as a possibility for play, but 
playgrounds and other play spaces, such as Tivoli and KUBE were mentioned.
•	
Play was primarily classified as social interaction and fun. This aligned with the definitions of play 
in the literature, which states; ”play is driven by intrinsic motivation and undertaken for its own sake, 
rather than as a means to an end” (UN Committee, 2013).
Image 4 - The children performing the play run activity during co-creation workshop.

4.4 Affinity Diagram
The most important findings from the fieldwork were gathered and structured into an affinity diagram to 
ensure that data was easy to apprehend later in the process (Martin & Hanington, 2012). The affinity dia-
gram is divided into seven overarching categories including play, development, interaction, play in the 
city, community, culture, and safety, see figure 15. Each of these categories contains the statements and 
insights gathered through the other methods used in the fieldwork. The affinity diagram was used as a 
tool in the ideation and development of concepts, as the preliminary data collected, created the overview 
needed for the development of the solution.

05
PROJECT SCOPE
==PAGE:57==
Integrate play into the urban environment of Copenhagen
05
Project Scope
The project scope section outlines and explain the intervention made on the city scale, when observed as 
a system. The purpose is to proivde an overview of the project, including its purpose, goals and conflicts, 
and to define how the project will be executed. 
5.1 Project Scope
In our investigations, we found that the reason for children refraining from playing outside of playgrounds 
in Copenhagen was due to a couple of different factors. These include the neighborhood culture, infra-
structure, and equipment available. This discovery proved that we found a problem, which was both very 
nuanced and complex. Blatantly, the problem lies in the need for a change in the social culture and, thus, 
the public opinion surrounding children’s play in the urban environment.
 
According to Gehl (1996), the rapid development of the city over the past decades means that buildings 
were built to contain people rather than for people. With this rapid development and expansion of the city, 
came a change in culture. The increase in population density, which amounts to a 37% increase from 1995 
to 2022 (Københavns Kommune, 2022), has affected how the city has developed. As the buildings have got-
ten taller, the feeling of community and neighborliness has gotten less (Gehl, 2010).
Image 5 - Copenhageners at Droning Louise’s Bro.
We found the statement confirmed through our initial fieldwork, where we observed the behaviors of  
Copenhageners with their children in the urban environment. The caregivers, especially, showed little 
to no engagement with fellow adults. While the children seemed curious about each other, they usually 
kept in close proximity to their guardians, which hindered them from interacting with other children 
(see section 4.2)
To envision how this problem could be solved, we have taken inspiration from The Impact Scale (Gehl, 
2010). This framework helps to explain how we aim to change the play culture through a curbside inter-
vention. But it also highlights what other initiatives could be taken at other intervention points to solve 
the same problem.
48
==PAGE:58==
Integrate play into the urban environment of Copenhagen
As illustrated by the figure, there is a possibility for intervention on the three levels; curbside, neighbor-
hood, and city scale. Depending on what level of intervention is investigated, a different solution would 
be necessary.
 
On the curbside level, the discovered issue was the lack of motivation to linger. Previously the pavement 
has been a gathering spot for people to meet but has been downgraded to the route leading from A to B 
(Jacobs, 1961). The problem could be solved through the implementation of artifacts, sculptures, or other 
means of beautification and interaction. Installing, e.g. artifacts on the curbside that encourage playful in-
teractions might contribute to facilitating social interactions in a diverse neighborhood and thereby create 
community cohesion (Bentway, 2021; Day, 2017). Furthermore, it is possible through art to create a space for 
exploration and self-development, in addition to providing a sense of belonging for children (Weitz, 1996).
On the neighborhood scale, the issue at hand is the lack of culture around letting children play on the 
street. To create this change in cultural behaviors, a community feeling would need to be developed. 
Examples of how this could be solved would be through events, communal projects, or other reasons 
for social gatherings. Lowe’s (2000) research found that by having neighborhood residents participate 
in community art, they were able to identify similar experiences and strengthen their feelings of solida-
rity by being able to voice and identify common concerns. 
49
Curbside
Neighborhood
City
Figure 16 - The Impact Scale (Gehl, 2010).
==PAGE:59==
Project Scope
50
Encouraging a community feeling has the possibility to lead to a change in the attitude of the adults, which 
could lead to children having more freedom and opportunity for play in public spaces (Churchman, 2003).
Lastly, on a city scale, regulations and strategies would need to be made and implemented in the ur-
ban development of cities. Through our interview with Copenhagen Municipality, (see section 4.1) 
we found that no regulations or strategies have been established to ensure that play is possible on a 
curbside level. The strategies would need to be changed also to encompass the children’s needs. Inter-
vening at the city scale would be a top-down approach to solving the problem. Although this appro-
ach would be useful and simplify the implementation process of installing artifacts, it is a tedious af-
fair, as the writing of strategies is tied to both municipal and governmental policies and policymakers. 
Without taking into account revised statutes or strategies, the construction of a playground may take 
up to 10 years, according to Helle Hagelund from the Municipality of Copenhagen (see section 4.1). 
To increase play in the city, it would thus be necessary to change the perception of children’s presence and 
movement in the public space.  
As explained, this culture change can happen through different outlets, with the above being pos-
sible examples. They would need further investigation as they are based on theoretical knowledge. 
The figure is based on the research and findings and is a snapshot of the situation during the term of the 
project and, why the impact of other future city development strategies has not been taken into account.
5.2 Problem Overview
The initial problem proposed was to integrate play outside of the playground. This led to the investigation 
of children’s play behavior and patterns in the urban environment of Copenhagen. Through this investiga-
tion, we realized that a cultural shift brought about by the city’s development was the true issue. With the 
public space no longer encouraging neighborhood interaction as it once did, the play was relocated from 
the street to the security of the fenced-in playground. 
The research revealed that children seldom left their immediate neighborhood without an adult unless, 
and only on commutes, such as to and from school, leisure activities, the grocery store, etc. The adult su-
pervision was heavily based on the safety concern of the child getting hurt by traffic. Consequently, the 
children have to be accompanied to and from the playground. This results in the play experience being 
less enjoyable for the child, due to the supervision while playing. 
We further found that children were enrolled in various extracurricular activities to fill their free time, 
as opposed to playing on playgrounds. The UN Committee views this as worrisome as it indicates that 
the children are not given enough time to play on their own terms (UN Committee, 2013). We thus saw 
an opportunity to develop a solution that would target children during these unsupervised moments. 
We aimed to solve the problem through intervention on the curbside level with a bottom-up approach. 
We believe that a systematic change in the play culture in Copenhagen can be effected through the inter-
vention of an artifact targeting children in the public space. As previously mentioned, this is simply one 
of many ways to address the issue. However, we believe this is where we can make the biggest impact 
on the systematic change, given our position, skill set, and expertise. Additionally, this aligns with Gehl’s 
approach to designing cities for people, where qualified decisions are made through the awareness of the 
individual’s utilization of the public space (Gehl, 2010).
==PAGE:60==
51
Integrate play into the urban environment of Copenhagen
*This observation is made from a Danish perspective. Cultural allowances for independence might differ.
Figure 17 - Problem overview.
We discovered that the ages seven to ten are a critical period in the child’s development. At this time, chil-
dren become more independent in terms of their allowance to roam the city*, social interactions are at 
their peak, and the interest in playgrounds is declining. This age range also represents a transitional stage 
before pre-adolescence, which is connected to a loss of interest in play, and compared to a younger target 
group, they are able to convey their wants and requirements (Castella, 2018). Based on these parameters, 
the target group was chosen. With the possibility of affecting this group’s sense of belonging in the city, 
we see a strong prospect for intervention. Implementing a solution designed for them could offer a greater 
sense of ownership in an environment planned and developed for adults. 
As the saying goes, Rome was not built in a day. A change in culture within a demographic will not either. 
Our decision to create a solution that fosters momentarily play is based on multiple factors. One is the de-
licate space of parental worry. As mentioned, we saw that parental concern for their children’s safety con-
sequently affects the children’s possibilities of exploring their local environment (see section 2.3.1 and 4.2).
Therefore, we see the need for a solution that does not keep the children lingering on their commutes. 
Furthermore, by encouraging short-term play, there is an opportunity for more users to engage with the 
solution. We see this as an opportunity, as the quantity of user interactions could contribute to enhancing 
the product’s appeal.
==PAGE:61==
52
Project Scope
5.3 The Development Arena
A development arena was created to map actors and artifacts that influence or can be influenced by the 
solution. It is worth noting that with the progression of the project, the arena has evolved. As can be deri-
ved from the map, there are many different categories within the project, with 10 major and several smaller 
ones. To illustrate their connection, they overlap each other. Given the project’s focus on the implementa-
tion of play in the urban environment, the cityscape is the largest of these categories. There, we observe a 
multitude of stakeholders and artifacts interacting, both on and through various constellations. 
 
Figure 18 - The development arena.
The main subject of the Children category was children who roam and interact with the urban space, main-
ly independently. Through our observation, and general social knowledge, we found that children begin 
to move through the city without a guardian at the ages of seven to eight. This independency depends on 
parental permission, which is often determined based on factors such as culture.
 
Before being granted permission, the children move with caregivers, another central category. The actors 
in this category strongly influence how the children interact with the city. We observed the children being 
shielded, controlled, or otherwise limited by parents, etc (see section 4.2).
 
Overall, we have two subcategories within Children; the independent child and the controlled child. The 
subcategory, independent child, contains; free and playful children. We observed that these behaviors 
were exhibited in familiar environments, such as at playgrounds, the schoolyard, parks, or on the commute 
to school. Our presumption is that this familiarity is gained from repeatedly walking the same route. This 
group of children showed trust and confidence in getting around.
Legal matters
Politicians
==PAGE:62==
53
Integrate play into the urban environment of Copenhagen
On the other hand, the controlled child was strongly connected to their primary caregivers. They exhibited 
more restraint and spent less time interacting with their surroundings. This was caused, in part, by the 
children being physically limited in their independent mobility by being carried on or strapped into cargo 
bikes, or held by the hand.
 
The role of authority of the caregivers greatly impacted the children’s interaction with the city. When in 
the company of an elder sibling, they showed less strict, curious behavior toward both the city and other 
children. When with parents, they showed more restraint, especially in their interaction with others. We 
generally observed that the parents physically shielded their children from the world, which led to the chil-
dren being limited in their playful interactions with the city. We presumed that this fear of the child getting 
hurt, can be projected onto the children, who thereby gain an insecure attachment to the environment.
5.4 Conflicts
We identified the four following conflicts in the development arena.  
Children & City scape
We discovered that children were not considered in urban development planning at a govern-
mental level. The strategies do not include children’s needs, which comes to show in how the 
city space has needed to be redesigned to provide space for the children. As a result, parks, 
squares, and playgrounds had to go through a makeover to accommodate the children (Appendix 03.9). 
Unfortunately, this has harmed the children’s sense of belonging to the city outside of the areas specifically 
designed for them. For instance, we found that the children we observed did not engage much in playful 
behaviors before getting to the schoolyard’s cobblestone.
Children, Caregivers & Traffic
We discovered a conflict between the traffic, the children, and their caregivers. Recent deca-
des have been spent improving the traffic conditions, leading to a less walkable city. Traffic 
signs, guard poles, street light, etc., has gradually encroached into the already narrow pave-
ments. As a result, children’s play is shielded from not only the traffic but also from the rest of the urban 
space. Moreover, as previously stated, we observed, that the caregivers physically shield the children. As 
a result, the parents gained control over their children’s movements. We suspect, that a significant factor 
in the parent’s need to restrain their children’s movement is their concern about traffic (see section 2.3.1). 
Fearing their child’s injury, they ended up restricting them from interacting with the city. 
 
With the parent’s precautions, the children receive little independence in their movements through the 
city. This is despite our findings, that children generally show greater caution on their own, compared to 
the adults we observed. It might be presumed, that the parents’ anxiety influences the children’s perceived 
safety in their environment. This presumption is based on the observations we made of the children’s he-
sitations in interacting with the city.
==PAGE:63==
54
Project Scope
We assume that the prevailing perception is, as Gehl (2010) explained it, that with the increase in traffic in 
the city, a rise in the frequency of accidents followed. And although heavy traffic might make the roads feel 
hazardous, the fact is, according to the Copenhagen Municipality, statistics show the contrary. The majori-
ty of the time, few accidents occur in densely trafficked areas (Appendix 03.9). Therefore, it can be argued 
that the perception of the risks by traffic might be inaccurate.
City Development & Regulations
When architects plan urban development, they are subjected to the legislation, rules, and 
regulations made by the state. This leads to a conflict between the two. In our interview with 
Copenhagen Municipality, we learned that, despite their desire, it is not feasible at a given 
time to plan the city with children in mind. Though their current strategies aim to embrace all citizens, they 
are aware of their shortcomings. As they are subjected to following these policies, the strategies would 
need altering to ensure that the children’s meets were met. 
Traffic & Events
The conflict between event and traffic stems from the traffic having to be stopped or deri-
ved for the activities to take place. This is evident during, amongst other events, the Copen-
hagen marathon. However, there is an opportunity, when the traffic is temporarily halted. 
At the half marathons, the locals usually make creative use of the closed streets. Without cars on the 
roads, they become a canvas for painting with chalk, which is seen as a communal activity to partici-
pate in. The events are good opportunities for altering people’s perception of the street as more than 
a pathway for transportation.
 
The imperative conflict in the project is the Children & Cityscape, which is considered to be the root cause 
of changing the culture of children’s play in the city. The behavior in public spaces cannot be changed if 
the children are not given the space and opportunity to play. Hence, it is not possible to start the virtuous 
cycle described by Gehl (1996). Adding additional curbside experiences might lead to the opportunity 
of recreating the use of the pavement as a gathering point. By solving the problem, the corresponding 
conflicts should be resolved, given their nature of being a symptom of the same problem (Norman, 2018). 
Creating a change in culture that emphasizes play on the streets might encourage a reduction in traffic, 
thus leading to a change in the parent’s perception of safety in the city.

key aspects of the problem space. This includes a definition of the requirement, the target group, and 
product placement for the final solution.

6.1 Persona
A persona was developed to acquire an understanding of the target group, and through that, make infor-
med decisions in relation to the project. The method was utilized as a supplementary tool for understan-
ding the target group’s behavior. To gather an in-depth understanding of the target group, a map of the 
children’s development was created. The map is based on the research done by Tilley, Anning, & Welles 
(2002) and Castella (2018), and highlights the three parameters, motor, cognitive, and social-emotional 
development. 
Figure 19 - Persona.
The persona is based on information acquired through desk research and fieldwork. The findings made it 
apparent that the ages seven to ten were particularly interesting for the project scope. This was, among 
other factors, because of the children’s newly gained independence for moving through the city unsuper-
vised. Another intriguing factor was that, in contrast to earlier stages of child development, where motor 
skills are predominated, the cognitive and social-emotional abilities were the ones in focus, see figure 3. 
==PAGE:66==
Integrate play into the urban environment of Copenhagen
57
Figure 20 - The child development stage at seven to ten years old.
As it is possible to deduce from the figure, the most dominant categories of development are cognitive 
and social-emotional skills. At this stage, children begin to learn and understand rules and develop logical 
thinking. Children become able to tell the difference between reality and illusion and start to understand 
the relationship between cause and effect.
 
At this point in the child’s development, the majority of the motor skills have matured, indicating that 
only fine motor skills are under development. Additionally, the social and interpersonal relationships are 
significant at this time, which comes to show with the children choosing social play interactions over inde-
pendent ones to a higher extent. The same conclusion was made in the co-creation workshop (see section 
3.4) which demonstrated that the enjoyment of the play was not determined by the activity, but rather by 
the quality of the interaction. 
Developmental stage at 7 to 10 years old
The model below was created to gain a better understanding of the target group’s level of development.
Developmental stage at 7 to 10 years old
When designing a solution, certain constraints are important to keep in mind. This includes the children’s 
ability to understand abstract thinking (Castella, 2018), and the rapidity of the child’s development, which 
differs from individual to individual. As previously mentioned (see section 2.2), children’s physical charac-
teristics and their social, logical, and cognitive intellect might differ substantially. 
Inclusivity beyond the target
One of the advantages of the selected target group is that a successful solution also would benefit a larger 
demographic, given the large difference in children’s development at the ages seven to ten. In this span, 
a wide range of abilities is developed within cognitive, social, and motor skills. As children’s development 
rate is not equal, two seven-year-olds might not share the skill sets and might also have extremely diverse

6.2 Product Requirements
The Product Requirement is based on the desk research and the findings acquired through fieldwork. Espe-
cially, the principles of inclusive- and universal design are embraced, as it has been important to develop a 
solution that can be utilized by a diverse range of citizens. Additionally, insights collected from experts during 
the fieldwork are used as a basis for the Product Requirement to meet the needs of stakeholders and users. 
 
The purpose of the Product Requirement is to get an overview of all the requirements and criteria the final 
solution has to fulfill, with the criteria being the measurements of success (Appendix 01.3). The factors 
outline the solution’s functionality, usability, design, etc.
Some of the requirements, Usability, and Inclusivity, have been emphasized in the section that follows, 
given their particular importance to the final solution.
Usability
The final solution targets children between the age of seven to ten. Therefore, it is required that the solu-
tion is suitable for the target group’s dimension and abilities, with a particular emphasis on their develop-
mental stage (see section 6.1). To accommodate the target group’s developmental stage, the functionality 
of the solution needs to be logical, to not lose the children’s interest immediately when interacting with 
the solution. This means that the solution must not break the framework that has been set (Appendix 03.8).
Additionally, the fieldwork showed that the type of play did not have much impact on the play experience, 
but was instead affected by the quality of social interaction (see section 4.3). Therefore, the solution must 
be usable by more than one user at a time, while not requiring multiple users in order to provide the chil-
dren with a playful experience when they move alone. 
Inclusivity
As the solution will be integrated into the public space, the principles of UD, have acted as a guideline that 
helps remove barriers to enable a wide range of citizens to participate equally, thus preventing feelings 
of exclusion from the community. The solution should accommodate citizens of various abilities and di-
mensions. A criterion is that citizens at a height of 95 cm should be able to use the solution, as it embraces 
children down to the age of three years old (Tilley, Anning, & Welles, 2002).
 
The solution must be intuitive in use and should not require instructions to understand how it is utilized, 
as to include as many citizens as possible regardless of their language barriers, reading ability, and con-
centration level. 
 
==PAGE:68==
Integrate play into the urban environment of Copenhagen
59
6.2.1 Black Boxed Requirements
Some factors have been necessary to black box, due to limited resources. These factors are included in 
the Product Requirement presented for future work, as they are crucial in the development of a successful 
solution, why, they must not be completely neglected. 
 
Quality
The quality of the solution determines whether the final solution can tolerate long time use in outdoor 
conditions. This is dependent on a variety of parameters, including material selection, construction robust-
ness, maintenance, and so on.
These parameters have not been investigated in the project, hence they can not be determined. Although, 
it is assessed that the solution should be kept with minimal to no maintenance. This requires that the so-
lution can cope with repeated use and tolerates seasonal changes with variations in temperature, wind, 
humidity, etc. The solution should at least be durable for three years in Danish weather conditions, where 
it is essential to consider that the cold and rainy months are predominant.
 Safety & Legal matter
Safety is, likewise, an important factor to include in the Product Requirement to ensure the user’s safety 
when using the final solution. The solution should not put any users at risk of injury or danger when utili-
zed.
 
Particularly, when targeting children and their play, the regulations and requirements become increasingly 
stringent. Playgrounds and equipment are required to follow Dansk Standard (Appendix 03.9), to ensure 
the children’s safety when playing. The Dansk Standard is a basic guideline and collection of standardiza-
tions and regulations (Dansk Standard, 2018), that a final solution characterized as play equipment has to 
follow to be implemented in Copenhagen Municipality.
Due to limited resources, it has not been investigated what the standard specifically entails, as it inclu-
des several factors related to manufacturing, construction, etc., which have not been determined for the 
final solution in this project. However, if the solution is not characterized as play equipment, but instead, 
as installation or sculpture, it is exempted from the Dansk Standard’s safety criteria, even though it may 
encourage playful interaction. Allowing for a variety of design opportunities, and enabling a more feasi-
ble implementation opportunity. This has been a consideration, which has affected the ideation process. 
Particularly, there has been a focus on installations, which can be composed of different elements such as 
sound, light, and texture, and is adapted to the environment in which it is placed. The interaction between 
the installation and the spectator is often emphasized.
  
The Dansk Standard’s regulations can, to some extent, be a barrier to the development of the final soluti-
on. Risk-taking is a key component of play. Children have the opportunity to explore risks and the conse-
quences when exposed to them as a part of a stimulating, challenging, and controlled environment, and 
it is considered greatly beneficial for development (Dansk Standard, 2017). Therefore, it is important that 
security and benefits are balanced. However, the safety of the children must be prioritized, even if it might
 
==PAGE:69==
Define
60
affect the play value. This relates to considerations of the environment and surroundings in which the 
solution is placed. It is necessary that the placement of the solution is protected from both bicycle and 
car traffic, but allows room for children to expand their playful behavior (Gehl Playscapes, n.d.). A factor, 
included in the Product Requirement, that addresses the safety issues, is Transparency, which states that 
the solution must be transparent to ensure that the surroundings have visibility of the child interacting 
with the solution.

6.3 Product Placement
The streets and their sidewalk have a big impact on the city with Jacobs (1961), even crediting them as 
the most vital organ of the city. The captivation of the streets is directly linked to the city and vice versa 
(Gehl, 2010). According to Jacobs (1961), the sidewalk has a significant role in the safety and assimilation of 
children, with the safest places for children to play being crowded neighborhood sidewalks. Research has 
shown that 70 percent of all life in the city takes place in the curb zones (København Kommune, 2018). The 
curbsides, therefore present an obvious placement opportunity for integrating play in the city. Focusing 
on larger sidewalks reduces the disturbance of the general flow of the city. This also excludes areas with 
the busiest traffic, e.g. Nørrebrogade, guaranteeing a perceived safer play experience.
 
In our observations, we found that children move independently between neighborhoods and schools, 
why this would be an advantageous placement for the solution. A small sample of the observed children 
also showed playful behavior interacting with the environment on these commutes.
 
Examples of specific areas that fit the requirements are:
Figure 23 - Solution placement.
Examples of specific areas that fit the requirements are: The sidewalk in front of Hellig Kors Kirke, as it is 
placed between a school, Blågård Skole and an after-school leisure-time club. As opposed to a typical 
two-lane sidewalk, this one is wider and situated in a less trafficked area. Our firsthand familiarity with the 
area lets us know that pedestrians avoid following the pavement, and rather stroll directly across the road. 
The solution would, therefore, not only be protected, but it might also influence other pedestrians’ move-
ments, fostering safer pedestrian traffic.
 
==PAGE:71==
Define
62
Another possibility would be by Korsgade 63, Nørrebro. At this location, the sidewalk is relatively large and 
in front of a residential area. The area is partly blocked by bicycle stands and thereby reasonably secured 
from traffic. Moreover, with the church, Hellig Kors Kirke, nearby, this location is also centered in between 
schools and institutions. excludes areas with the busiest traffic, e.g. Nørrebrogade, guaranteeing a percei-
ved safer play experience.
 
In our observations, we found that children move independently between neighborhoods and schools, 
why this would be an advantageous placement for the solution. A small sample of the observed children 
also showed playful behavior interacting with the environment on these commutes. 
Figure 24 - Solution placement at Korsgade 63, Nørrebro.
Figure 25 - The curbside zone in an area of Nørrebro marked with white.
