CONCEPT DEVELOPMENT
==PAGE:73==
07
Concept Development 
The chapter provides a description of the ideation process that has been inspired, amongst others, by 
the children’s contribution throughout the co-creation. The ideation process resulted in six overall design 
concepts that are described briefly. 
Integrate play into the urban environment of Copenhagen
64
7.1 Results from co-creation
A co-creation workshop was held with the target group as a means to develop a solution based on a qu-
alified foundation. Our research claimed, that children are seldom advised, nor get the chance to voice 
their wants and needs when urban areas are designed and developed, even when intended for them (see 
section 4.1). Thus we found it necessary to involve them in the design process. Following a UCD approach., 
led to a greater possibility of avoiding repeating failures, as the exclusion of children. The involvement 
ensured that we fulfilled the target groups’ actual needs.
The workshop created an opportunity to empower the target group to take part in the design process. 
With more time available, it would have been beneficial for the project to include more stakeholders in 
the ideation process. An interesting proposition would be to invite stakeholders from different fields, to 
inspire, cooperate and co-create together. Given the children’s expertise in their play, they were prioritized.
Their insights provided different perspectives on play and a better understanding of the factors that make 
play enjoyable. 
 
The co-creation workshop was divided into two main sessions, the first part described in a previous sec-
tion (see section 4.3). The second part’s objective was to engage the target group to ideate and develop 
concepts. The concepts represented what they wanted to play with on a daily commute. To create an ima-
ginative setting, they were asked to pretend they were going home from school and crossing a new play 
item along the way.
12 creations were made by the 17 present students (Appendix 05.9). They were allowed to group up, to 
ensure that they could inspire each other and felt comfortable. The children were encouraged to explain 
their concepts and share their thoughts on the key elements, either verbally or through cartoons. This was 
an attempt to accommodate the children’s abilities and ensure several communication opportunities.
Image 6 - Some of the creations presented by the students.
Creation 1
Made by two girls
Creation 3
Made by two girls
Creation 2
Made by a boy
==PAGE:74==
65
Integrate play into the urban environment of Copenhagen
The main findings from the analysis of the children’s creations are as follows: 
•	
Nearly all the creations embraced social interactions. This aligned with the findings from the first 
part of the workshop (see section 4.3). Examples of social interactive solutions were slides for ra-
cing against each other and a camera recording people’s movements on the other side of the world. 
•	
Competition was a repeating element in the children’s creations. At this age, the children en-
joy challenges and are able to engage in competition and follow rules (Castella, 2018), and they 
find it particularly enjoyable, as it leads to feelings of accomplishment or success from trying. 
•	
The target group had a lot of different interests, that they practiced after school, which was refle-
cted in their solutions.  It is worth noting, that their interest varied significantly, despite sharing 
age and area of residency. 
•	
The target group had not developed a mature level of abstract thinking. Resulting in many of the 
children’s creations resembling known objects, that might be found at a playground. However, some 
creations held unpredictable elements, such as a teddy bear doubling as a vending machine.
7.2 Ideation process
Our ideation process led to ideas and solutions generated through sessions with mind mapping (Appen-
dix 02.2) and brainstorming techniques. The information acquired throughout the project influenced the 
outcome and resulted in the process being done iteratively. The outcome of the process was six concepts 
derived from over 50 distinct ideas, totaling 11 separate design proposals.
Initially, the information gathered throughout the project was collected and sorted in the Affinity Diagram, 
see figure 15. The key statements were then placed in the Mind Map to be further ideated on. These state-
ments include, amongst others; ”Play starter”, ”Feeling of success”, ”Use of the environment”, and ”Change 
in trends”. By allowing us to draw inspiration from the data gathered, the mind map marked the beginning 
of the ideation process, as it aided in structuring and exploration. Ideas that sprung from the mindmap 
included; guiding paths to learn how to move in the city, lines guiding from a to b, and vegetables for a 
playful learning experience.
 
To further ideate, several brainstorming sessions were conducted. These sessions contained different met-
hods and techniques including, silent and collective brainstorming, to ensure that no ideas were lost in the 
process. The aim of the silent brainstorming was not to influence each other and thus broaden the solution 
space, and the collective’s was to inspire new ideas. The ideas were reviewed and clustered afterward, crea-
ting different categories of ideas. This proved to be the ”optimal” framework for producing as many ideas 
as possible within a short time frame.
==PAGE:75==
66
Concept Development
Three keywords were identified; Simple, Feedback, and Installations, creating categories under which the 
ideas were clustered. This created the foundation for another session of brainstorming. By creating catego-
ries, the aim var diverged, thus narrowing down the framework to ideate within. The new ideas generated 
were then clustered with similar ideas and possible combinations, see figure 26. These were the basis for 
further concept development, which resulted in eight conceptual ideas.
  
Each of the clusters was further investigated with various design proposals, varying in shape, size, and fun-
ction, thus creating multiple proposals within one concept. This led to a collection of 11 different design 
proposals. Drawings were created to describe possible features, functionality, play principles, etc. see figure 
27 and 28. 
 
Through continuous learning, the design proposals have undergone multiple iterations, where they have 
been reevaluated and redesigned as new knowledge has been gathered.
Figure 26 - Brainstorming session based on the keywords; installation, simple, feedback (light).
==PAGE:76==
67
Integrate play into the urban environment of Copenhagen
7.3 Concept Development
In the ideation process, we developed multiple ideas that all sought to solve the problem. This led to six 
concepts with eleven different design proposals. The concepts are briefly described;
Stepping stone
The Stepping Stones are pathways that aim to direct or encourage different movements. They 
have a texture, shape, etc., that differs from the pavement. While interacting with the pathway, 
the user can receive feedback such as light, sound, colors, patterns, etc., to enhance the enjoy-
ment of the play.
Light Strip
The Light Strip is a strip of light integrated along or in between the pavement, and it is con-
trolled by interaction. These interactions could be the speed of the light controlled through 
repeating pressure or the universal game of don’t step on the crack.
Light Poles
The concept encompasses light poles that are scattered around, making a path where the 
light can be turned off and on by pushing a button on the poles. The light can both be turned 
on and off randomly or systematically in different colors or create different patterns on the 
ground.
Swirly Rail
The concept is a rail that is in different shapes, which encourages various interactions. These 
interactions include encouraging movement along the way or more static interactions, such 
as climbing or using it as a hangout spot.
Interactive Board
The board is covered in switches. The switches react when the user interacts with them, which 
allows the user to leave a mark on the environment, like writing a message, creating an image, 
etc. Different games could be incorporated, such as whack-a-mole or memory games. 
Playful Installations
The playful installations aim to encourage activity by being changeable through interactions, 
leading to people having the power to change the appearance of the city. The interaction 
could be by turning plates, having light illuminate when passing through arches, and more.  
==PAGE:77==
68
Concept Development
Figure 27 - Sketching of the installation concepts.
==PAGE:78==
08
CONCEPT EVALUATION
==PAGE:79==
Integrate play into the urban environment of Copenhagen
70
08
Concept Evaluation
In this section, we will conduct a thorough evaluation of the 11 concepts. The objective is to provide a 
comprehensive analysis that will enable us to make informed decisions about the choice of concept.
8.1 Decision Matrix
A Decision Matrix was utilized to decide which of the concepts should be further developed, prototyped, 
and tested. The purpose of the method is to compare the concept proposal to the established criteria, thus 
leading to well-considered decisions. The matrix has two axes, imposing as the criteria; play potential and 
feasibility.
 
Play potential refers to the possibility for alternative play options within a single solution, as well as the 
duration of which the solution experience will continue to be enjoyable for the target group.
 
Feasibility represents the possibility of developing, creating and testing the solution within the given time 
frame and availability of resources. This criterion also refers to the possibility of implementation without 
disturbing the environment and flow of the city. As a consequence, the concept proposals have been pla-
ced on the matrix, with the previously specified placement in mind (see section 6.3).
Figure 28 - Some of the concepts evaluated by the pedagogues. 
To get a better grasp of the possible play potential of each of the concepts, two of the previously inter-
viewed pedagogues were advised. This dialogue was considered a minimum viable product test, why it is 
further explored in a later section. The pedagogues’ insights, understanding of children’s play, and sugges-
tions gave us new ideas for the concepts and provided us with an understanding of what elements might 
be necessary to keep the children intrigued time after time of use. 
==PAGE:80==
71
Integrate play into the urban environment of Copenhagen
Three design proposals were selected using the matrix. They were chosen as they offered the most poten-
tial for the implementation of various play potentials within the solution, and because of their feasibility 
in being testable and able to be implemented in the designated areas of our choosing. Additionally, we 
found that the three options had the best chance of meeting the product specifications (Appendix 01.3). 
 
The larger installations, as can be deduced, are situated in the matrix’s lower quarter. These had the least 
potential as they did not fulfill the criteria. They proved to have the least play potential, given our target 
group’s need for interaction and feedback, as pointed out by the pedagogues consulted (see section 8.4). 
Furthermore, it was observed that the proposals would be challenged in trying to achieve the product 
requirements, which impacted the likelihood of the solutions being feasible. Especially the criteria of trans-
parency and avoiding inconvenience for others would be hard to fulfill with the larger installations.
Figure 29 - Decision Matrix.
==PAGE:81==
72
Concept Evaluation
8.2 Iterations
The decision matrix underwent a number of iterations as the design proposals were positioned in accor-
dance with our gathered knowledge, expectations, and experience. The iterations were mainly based on 
the experiences we had while developing and planning the prototypes for testing.
  
One of the discoveries made was that the rail proved too complicated an installation than we initially 
anticipated. To test the principle, which was to climb, sit and hang out on, it would require a certain dura-
bility, which we did not have the resources available to ensure. This gave us the indication that the rail was 
positioned too high on feasibility compared to other proposals. This made room for other solutions, such 
as the Interactive Board, which was moved up significantly, making it one of the solutions to be further 
developed and investigated.  
8.3 The Three Selected Concepts
Three concepts were selected using the Decision Matrix. They all share some of the same game principles, 
as well as sharing other parameters, such as fostering the same developmental skills. Memory and se-
quence recognition are game principles that are repeated in all of the selected concepts, as this practice 
supports the development of cognitive skills, providing the children with better short-term memory skills. 
These practices are important, as they align with the target group’s developmental stage, where cognitive 
development is at its peak (see section 6.1), and fostering cognition leads to better academic development 
(Gleave & Hamilton, 2012). 
Some of the feasible games encourage psychical activity, such as wack-a-mole, which can support motor 
development with skills like coordination, strength, and balance. All the concepts can be played alone or 
in interaction with others in games that are either cooperative, collaborative, or competitive. This strengt-
hens the child’s social understanding and -skills, which is also particularly important for the target group. 
 
The following is a detailed description of the three concepts and how these differ from each other in sup-
porting the child’s development. Furthermore, each of the concepts is labeled with play type and practices 
identified in the co-creation workshop. 
Concept 1 - The Interactive Board
The Interactive Board is a bigger installation that invites citizens to interact. The board has several devel-
opment opportunities, that can vary from the user having to turn a switch on and off or flip fields to create 
a pattern or message. Through interaction with the board, the citizen has the opportunity to leave a mark 
in the urban space. The next time the citizen passes the board, others’ interactions might have changed or 
modified the appearance of the board, thus creating an element of surprise.
This can assist in fostering a sense of community as the citizens can interact with the board collaboratively. 
The concept can double as an interactive game with different game opportunities, like four in a row, wack-
a-mole, path memorization, etc. The games have the potential to be of various degrees of difficulty to 
appeal to different age groups. This can encourage competitive and collaborative interactions with peers, 
or across generations.
==PAGE:82==
73
Integrate play into the urban environment of Copenhagen
Several of the play practices identified at the co-creation workshop can be applied to the concept, this 
includes, interactive, social, competition, and collaboration.
In interaction with the concept’s switches, the child’s motor skills can be challenged to improve develop-
ment. More specifically, the child must use fine motor skills, as the interaction with the switches requires 
a certain precision. With creativity, the child can create all kinds of patterns or messages. This supports 
the child in expressing themselves artistically and articulating their feelings and thoughts in the public 
environment. 
Concept 2 - The Stepping Stone
The Stepping stone encourages citizens to step on the paths, these paths can have the appearance of a 
raised stone or otherwise differ from the pavement on which it is placed. As citizens interact with the ele-
ment, it activates feedback, such as light or sound. This allows for the opportunity of different games such 
as races, follow a path, minesweeper, etc. Through the use of different colors, the user interacting with the 
path can be challenged on different levels of difficulty. Based on the description, the following play prac-
tices could be applied to the concept; interactive, social, and competition.
The interactions with the stepping stone enable the children to develop their motor skills, as they would 
be required to jump or climb between the stones. Engaging the children in physical activities disguised as 
play leads to an increase in strength, coordination, balance, and well-being. Additionally, it is feasible to 
challenge the cognitive sense, in the same manner as described in the previous concept, by integrating 
similar games.
Concept 2 - The Light Poles
The Light Poles are several poles placed with distance, creating a path. Each pole is a source of light that can be 
turned on and off by the push of a button. As the citizens push the button, the light is activated systematically 
or randomly, meaning one light pole’s button can turn on or off another pole’s light. This can create different 
types of play interacting with the Light Poles, with games previously mentioned such as, races, wack-a-mole, 
path memorization, etc. This concept also allows for competition or collaborations between different users. 
The play practices that can be applied to the concept are; interaction, social, competition, collaboration, 
and unpredictability.
==PAGE:83==
74
Concept Evaluation
Similarly to the previous concepts, in the interaction with the Light Poles, the child’s motor skills are de-
veloped, in particular the gross motor skills, with the child physically moving between the poles. As the 
light can be turned on and off in a systematic pattern, it can challenge the child’s logical thinking and 
understanding of the relationship between cause and effect, which is a cognitive skill that is developed at 
the stage of the target group.
 
Each concept would be able to satisfy the requirements of the final solution. Therefore, we found it crucial 
to assess the solutions with the target group and examine their interest in the proposed solutions.
8.4 Minimum Viable Product Testing
To evaluate the concepts’ function and to obtain feedback from the end users and other relevant stakehol-
ders, a test of the minimum viable product was conducted. The testing was done before and after selecting 
the three concepts. This allowed for testing the children’s interest in one of the potential prototypes and 
professional evaluation from pedagogues. The intended goal was to specify important qualities found in 
all three prototypes and to clarify what solution should be the final.
8.4.1 Feedback From Dialogue With Pedagogues
We brought the 11 design proposals to an open discussion with Christian and Louise, two pedagogues, 
who had previously been interviewed, in order to gather expert feedback. The pedagogues’ opinions and 
experience led to new knowledge and idea development when they identified multiple opportunities for 
different proposals.
A significant insight gained from their feedback was that various age groups have varied interests, hence 
they would have different demands from interactions. While younger children would be satisfied with 
simply exploring a solution without feedback, older children need more interactive solutions that en-
courage competition and socializing.
In Christian’s opinion, the children would easily feel intrigued to interact with all the proposed solutions, 
given that the game principles seemed recognizable in all of them. The final solution, therefore, needs a 
sufficient degree of visual recognition.
The feedback did not provide a definite answer as to which of the concepts was better, comparatively, but 
instead led to a discussion of what elements are crucial for the optimal play experience. As a result, we 
decided to evaluate the concepts on other parameters, as previously mentioned, see figure 29.
8.4.2 Testing Play Principles
The purpose of the testing was to gain a better understanding of the play potential and key characteristics 
of each of the three concepts, thus helping to decide which concept to develop further.
 
A low-fidelity prototype was developed to simulate the concepts’ game principles. As a result of the MVP 
principle fail fast, fail cheap, the prototypes were produced from inexpensive and available resources. This 
enabled an opportunity for quickly creating and evaluating prototypes, thus learning from the process 
and potential failures.
==PAGE:84==
75
Integrate play into the urban environment of Copenhagen
The three prototypes were all tested simultaneously, in the same location and under the same conditions 
(Appendix 06.7-06.9). The prototypes were placed outside of Klub Filippa as the children arrived at the insti-
tution after school. Each prototype was accompanied by a sign with illustrations that represented several 
play suggestions, see image 7. This allowed the children’s interactions with the prototype to be observed 
from a distance without causing unnecessary interference. 
The length of time the children spent interacting with the prototype was used as an indicator of their level 
of interest, and the play potential was observed by the children’s interactions. Lastly, their engagement 
was used to identify interactions that differed from the predicted use. 
Image 7 - Play suggestions for the MVP prototype testing.
Image 8 - MVP prototype testing in front of Fritids- Junior- og Ungdomsklubben Filippa.
==PAGE:85==
76
Concept Evaluation
Results
The goal was to observe the children’s interactions with the prototype and thus determine which concept 
had the most traction. This, however, was not the outcome. We observed that the prototypes went unno-
ticed, which may be a result of a variety of reasons:
•	
The children appeared to be focused on getting to Klubben Filippa and were not aware of their sur-
roundings. They showed no signs of veering off the path connecting the school and club.
•	
The prototypes were visually too simple; the fact that the function worked was not enough to capture 
the children’s interest. This might be a result of the level of abstraction at this stage of development, 
see figure 3. 
•	
The area in front of Klub Filippa did not seem like a space the children felt comfortable claiming, 
without an adult present, as seen when a pedagogue went outside of the border of the club. His pre-
sence gave the children the confidence to flock outside on the pavement. This conclusion is in line 
with previous findings, which showed that children only begin playful behavior in safe settings.
•	
 Few children engaged with the prototype, but it usually resulted in other children shooing them 
away. Indicating that, despite the signs encouraging interactions, they felt uncomfortable dealing 
with something unknown.
Learnings
Despite the test not procuring the anticipated results, we found an opportunity to learn from the errors 
and observations we made. The majority of the children did not spend time reading the instructions and 
game suggestions, which indicated that a play starter was needed to entice interactions with the prototy-
pes. The light poles, the most visible of the prototypes, were also the only ones that garnered significant 
attention, which led to the conclusion of how crucial visibility is for the solution to encourage play.
 
The results of the test were impacted by a variety of factors. The duration of the testing session was one. 
We anticipate that the children would have behaved differently, ideally, more explorative if they had been 
left for a longer period of time. The children were shooing each other away from the prototypes, which 
signals that they were cautious of getting reprimanded for engaging with it. 
 
Regardless of the absence of anticipated interactions, we were able to identify the advantages and disad-
vantages of each of the concepts. We discovered that the final solution can not occupy too much space, 
through our testing, as bicycles had trouble passing the prototypes. However, the solution still needs to be 
visible to grab the children’s attention. Additionally, we found that the solution needs to have an obvious 
play starter to communicate the playability without instructions.
==PAGE:86==
77
Integrate play into the urban environment of Copenhagen
8.5 Selected Concept
Throughout the ideation process, it became clear the problem can be solved in various ways, the final con-
cept selected is, therefore, just one of many solutions. Furthermore, if the problem was to be resolved at 
a different level (see section 5.1), it would need other approaches or interventions that are not within the 
scope of this project.
 
The selection of the final concept was based on the opportunity for the fulfillment of several criteria. As the 
MVP test did not provide insight into the preference of the target group, the selection was based on the 
following factors; understanding the target group’s needs from previous work, the various stakeholders’ 
input, product and placement requirements, and a professional assessment based on gathered 
knowledge.
As the solution aims to be implemented on curbsides in areas of children’s daily traffic, a crucial factor has 
been that the solution must not conflict with the traffic flow in the urban space. Meaning that the solution 
can not occupy too much space on the curbside. The first concept, the interactive board, comparatively 
best fulfilled these criteria, as the solution is horizontal rather than vertical. However, as the MVP test reve-
aled, the solution need to be noticeable to ensure engagement from children (see section 8.4).
 
The game principles constitute a significant role for whom the solution appeals, where the board is supe-
rior, as it is presumed to have more play opportunities, as the play principles from the other two concepts 
could be implemented. However, the physical activity would be isolated to a smaller area. It should be 
noted that the interactions with the solution should not disturb the environment in which it is placed, but 
this will be explored later.
Figure 30 - Comparison between the three concepts.
In the conversation with the pedagogues, it was mentioned that the target group would become disinte-
rested in the interactions with the stepping stone more rapidly compared to the other two concepts. They 
believed that the solution would be more appreciated by a younger audience, who might be entertained 
simply by the simplicity of the design. To preserve the target groups’ interest, the solution needs another 
level of complexity and encourage social interactions, like competition and cooperation as identified
==PAGE:87==
78
Concept Evaluation
as key elements to a successful play experience. Both the Light Poles and the Interactive Board have the 
potential to fulfill this need. 
 
Of the three solutions, the Interactive Board is the only concept that enables the children to have an im-
pact on their environment. Throughout the project, it was evident how crucial it is for children to claim 
urban spaces and be recognized as fellow citizens (see section 2.3.1). The ability for children to leave their 
mark on the urban environment is therefore considered a significant opportunity for the integration of 
them and their play into the city.
Initiatives by the children, such as small artworks, can make the curbside more fascinating and can foster 
other social interactions on the curbside level. Additionally, the Interactive Board is believed to support 
and encourage playful interactions cross-generational, which both the municipality of Copenhagen and 
Kompan mentioned is a need that is currently unfulfilled in the city.
==PAGE:88==
09
PROTOTYPE DEVELOPMENT
==PAGE:89==
09
Prototype Development
Three distinct prototypes were developed; a physical, a digital, and a design proposal. The collected objec-
tive was to create two functional models, that could be tested for proof of concept, and to test and decide 
on different design proposals in collaboration with the target group.  
Integrate play into the urban environment of Copenhagen
80
Given the children’s availability, as we took them from class, the testing of all three prototypes was conduc-
ted simultaneously. The sixteen students were split into two groups of six and one of four, with each group 
having twenty minutes to complete all three rounds of testing. When the children arrived in their groups, 
they were separated into two groups and sent to either the physical or the digital prototype testing. After 
trying both, they were then brought together to decide and develop in collaboration with us, the appea-
rance of the prototype. 
The prototype was tested on the children, who previously participated in our co-creation workshop, the 
fourth grade from Skolen ved Søerne. The goal was to observe and learn from the children’s interactions 
with the prototypes, thus helping us create a foundation for our decisions.
9.1 Physical Prototype
To test the idea for proof of concept, it was required to construct a physical prototype. This would provide 
us with the opportunity to gain direct and honest feedback from our target group. The mechanical proto-
type had to simulate the functionalities intended for the final solution, in order to assess the target group’s 
interactions and whether the solution contributed with an entertainment value.
Following an MVP strategy, a mechanical prototype was selected, as it could be rapidly and cheaply crea-
ted with the available resources. After the initial MVP testing (see section 8.4), we discovered that the so-
lution needed to be more complex and eye-catching to capture the interest of our target group. Thus we 
decided to create a solution that incorporated a light element that could be controlled, as it could act like 
feedback during the interaction.
9.1.1 Description
The mechanical prototype consisted of MDF sheets in the dimensions 650mm in height times 500mm in 
width, which was laser cut into the desired shape, see image 9. The front sheet had 6x6 circles and cutouts 
big enough to fit the opening and closing mechanism. This simulated the light switches, allowing the user 
to create patterns or letters. A LED strip was placed inside of the board, peaking out of the circles, when 
the gate was opened up. With a remote, the light could be changed into a variety of colors and nuances. To 
reflect the light, aluminum foil was placed behind the opening and closing mechanism. To ensure that the 
prototype would be able to be placed on a table, thus suitable for children’s height, two feet were created 
to stabilize it. Lastly, the board was cut in an organic shape in order to create a more finished look, as well 
as make it more appealing to the children. 
==PAGE:90==
Integrate play into the urban environment of Copenhagen
81
Image 9 - The physical prototype.
9.1.2 Other Prototype Development Possibilities
When developing the physical prototype, several other development possibilities were considered. A se-
mi-digital prototype that required Arduino and programming was assumed the most ideal for testing a 
prototype that included the function of games, as these could be programmed into the light switches. 
However, this was assessed to be too time-consuming and costly, given the time and resources available. 
It was also considered to use bigger lights that could be turned on and off by push. This would require a 
much bigger board, as the dimension of the lights that we could get a hold of had an average diameter 
of 100 mm. The lights were also expensive, as the solution required a minimum of 36, and ordering bulk 
was deemed too risky, as it was presumed that it would not arrive within the time frame for development. 
Furthermore, it was questioned whether the light sources were strong enough to light up in daylight and, 
thereby, fulfill the intended function. 
On this basis, it was determined that the MVP approach would be the most viable solution to rapidly create 
a mechanical prototype and test the solution with our target group.
9.1.3 Testing
The physical prototype was tested as part of the proof of concept. The overall purpose of the test was to 
determine if the children were entertained by the board. This was measured by observing whether it kept 
their interest during the test. We were interested in seeing and experiencing the children’s initial reactions 
and interactions with the prototype, however, given their hesitation, this was not possible.
==PAGE:91==
Prototype Development
82
The children showed much care for the prototype in the beginning and asked for allowance to interact.
During the test, the children were facilitated through two tasks; (1) remembering the pattern made by 
another child and recreating it in collaboration, and (2) creating a letter for the next group to recognize. 
This was done to simulate the game principles on the board and test if the functioning made logical sen-
se for them, or if it was too abstract (see section 2.2 and 6.1). By asking them to create letters, we directly 
encouraged them to try one of the key features of the final prototype, testing if it was possible for the 
children to create letters with a sequence of dots. 
Image 10 - Testing the physical prototype on the target group.
To create a collaborative experience, the children who made the pattern were allowed to change the color 
of the LED strip to red, when the child recreating it made an error. This helped them assist each other in 
creating the correct pattern, and it provided visual feedback. Given the limited time available for testing, 
the children had to decide who made the pattern and who recreated it, as they would not be able to take 
turns. After each pattern was created and photographed, all doors were pushed shut, creating a blank 
slate, for the child to recreate it. Since only one look at the photographed pattern was insufficient, the chil-
dren were allowed to see it numerous times.
==PAGE:92==
9.1.4 Results
The following objectives were to be met when testing the prototype;
1. If the children intuitively understood how to interact with the board 
2. If the children interacted with the board differently than anticipated
3. If the board proved to be entertaining
4. If the game principles could be utilized when interacting with the board
Because the children did not interact with the board on their own initiative, the 1st and 2nd objectives 
could not be confirmed.
The children were surprisingly good at collaborating on each task and gave each other space to interact 
with the board. Some of the children were faster at figuring out the mechanism than others. Again proving 
that children are very different at this stage.  All the children understood how to interact with the board, 
some were not afraid of trying or asking, while others felt more comfortable observing the others’ intera-
ctions first.
83
Integrate play into the urban environment of Copenhagen
Figure 31 - The test results collected from testing the physical prototype.
==PAGE:93==
84
Prototype Development
The main findings from the tests were:
•	
The children were entertained by the solution. The indicator of this was that all the children kept 
their interest in the interactions during the test period. Furthermore, a majority of the children also 
expressed disappointment when they had to stop, as they wanted to continue playing. 
•	
Some of the children were intrigued simply by the mechanism of the prototype, as they could 
have spent a long time just opening and closing the gates. In two cases where the children were 
asked to create a pattern, it was necessary to rush them to ensure enough time to complete the 
test.
•	
The children were easily able to create and recognize letters. They started out with simple letters, 
such as I and L, and then changed to more challenging letters, such as Æ, and Ø. All the groups 
were able to identify what letter the other group had made.
•	
The size of the board worked well for two children collaborating, but anything past was too exces-
sive with the amount of space available. 
•	
Other children, both ones performing the other test and children passing by, showed interest and 
curiosity in the interactions with the prototype.
•	
The prototype was very noticeable due to the LED light, as it was bright and able to change colors, 
it is presumed to have added to the appeal.
9.1.5 Limitations
During the testing of the prototype, we experienced some limitations. The children’s confidence in en-
gaging with the prototype might have been impacted by our observations of their behavior. This was 
demonstrated by their apprehension to interact with the prototype, and how the majority sought allow-
ance to engage. While the observer tried to remain neutral, they responded to the children’s questions, 
to ensure that they felt comfortable. This meant that the children’s initial interactions with the prototype 
could not be determined, and the outcome is, therefore, a reflection of the interactions being facilitated. 
It cannot be determined whether the children would have been equally entertained if they were to play 
freely without facilitation.
It can be assumed that the result and the perceived entertainment value have been influenced by the 
children’s collaborative, thus social interactions, and this aligns with our previous findings. However, some 
children could immerse in creating patterns and letters for a considerable amount of time without others 
being present.
==PAGE:94==
In relation to the mechanical limitations, we found that the opening and closing mechanisms of the pro-
totype did not function as smoothly as desired. This proved a challenge for some children when creating 
patterns, as they did not want to use force and risk breaking the prototype. The circles were larger than 
anticipated for the final solution, making it challenging to recognize the letters up close, but it proved not 
to be an issue when stepping back. As a result, the children had to step back several times to ensure that 
their letter was constructed correctly. 
9.2 Digital Prototype
To simulate the digital games, that the solution would be able to facilitate, we created a digital prototype 
of the game whack-a-mole and a pattern memory game. The prototype was developed in Figma, which 
is a collaboratory web application for interface design. The program allowed us to create every wireframe 
and the interactions between them, which provided us with full control of the functions that would be 
required for the test. 
The prototype was created in Danish, as it was assumed that the native language of the children would 
be most suitable, given the uncertainty surrounding their English skills. However, the amount of written 
explanation was reduced, and instead, other tools were used to make the game more intuitive. This was 
done by creating an avatar that served as a guide. 
To adhere to the MVP method, the prototype’s function was relatively simple, this allowed us to develop it 
rapidly, allowing us to learn from it fast and identify failures that could occur throughout the test. As pre-
viously mentioned, the target group found it difficult to interact with concepts if they were too simple or 
abstract, meaning that appearance is an important aspect of the experience (see section 8.4). As a result, 
the game had a theme; in this case, UFOs and aliens, which was inspired by the children’s own inventions 
from the co-creation workshop (see section 7.1). 
9.2.1 Description
The landing page consisted of a menu with two options, asking to choose a game; Catch the Light or Me-
mory Game.  Catch the Light was an interpretation of the game whack-a-mole, where the goal was to catch 
the most amounts of lights, with a total of 12 being catchable. The light was set to pick up the speed, with a 
beginning delay of 2000ms between the wireframes and a final delay of 1000ms, thus increasing the level 
of difficulty.
Every time the light was caught, a motivational message would appear covering the screen accompanied 
by a character. This served as feedback, letting the children know that they had achieved the objective. Gi-
ven that the light was not captured, no messages would appear, and after the determined delay, it would 
switch to the next wireframe.
Integrate play into the urban environment of Copenhagen
The objective of the Memory Game was to recall the correct sequence of lights. The game began with dis-
playing a text instructing the player to look closely, as a certain sequence was demonstrated, and required 
to be imitated. Two separate levels of difficulty were created, substantiating the children’s identified need 
for a challenge (Appendix 03.5). 
85
==PAGE:95==
Prototype Development
86
Image 11 - Wireframes of the digital prototype.
The first level consisted of four fields, with four lighting up in a pattern as seen in and the second had nine 
fields, where five lit up in another alternative pattern, see image 11. On both levels, the first light was being 
pointed out by a character, as to aid and explain the concept without written or verbal instructions. In 
terms of feedback, the Memory Game was a contrast to the other game, as it operated oppositely. It uses 
feedback for unfavorable interactions rather than giving feedback for the intended ones. When the chil-
dren entered the wrong sequence, they would be sent to a wireframe that asked them to try again. 
9.2.2 Testing
Testing the digital prototype was one part of testing for the proof of concept. The purpose was to deter-
mine whether the children enjoyed the game principles, what level of challenge would be required, and 
whether they found it acceptable as a social interaction. 
The children were introduced to the game and were told to just give it a try and see if they could figure 
it out on their own. They were also informed that they were allowed to freely ask any questions as well as 
voice any comment or discuss the game with one another.
Our assumptions were that the children would relatively easily be able to navigate the game, given that 
there were only a few buttons to press and characters highlighting the initial actions that had to be taken. 
The presumption proved accurate, but on different grounds than anticipated. The children learned how to 
navigate the game through collaboration, and trial and error, and showed little interest in reading the few 
instructions available. 
==PAGE:96==
9.2.3 Results
The following objectives were to be met when testing the pro-
totype;
1. If the level of difficulty matched the need of the target group
2. If the solution satisfied the need for social connection
3. If the solution was entertaining
The children’s differences in cognitive ability to recall the se-
quence in the memory game and their difference in motor skills 
when having to catch the lights in the game of the same name, 
were extremely evident. This support previous findings, which 
highlight how much the children differ from each other in this 
development stage. As a result, some children found the games 
more challenging, and others quickly understood and comple-
ted the objectives, and asked for more challenging levels.
The children who found the games the most challenging were assisted by their classmates, who showed 
great patience while letting their peers try to complete the objectives. This demonstrated that it is possi-
ble to promote social interactions between children by providing games such as the ones present in the 
digital prototype.
Last but not least, we interpreted the children’s inquiries about more levels as interest in continuing to 
play, thus an indication that they found it entertaining. However, it would be a requirement that the levels 
changed frequently, in order for the children to keep their interest.
In addition, we found the following; 
87
Integrate play into the urban environment of Copenhagen
•	
The children had trouble concentrating on the digital prototype because they could watch what 
happened in the testing of the physical prototype. Even with the computer being the initial most 
alluring, they quickly lost interest when they watched their peers sprint back and forth. This indi-
cated that the children found the aspect of physical activity interesting.
•	
The children were truly fascinated by the realization that we created the prototypes ourselves and 
showed great excitement for the opportunity to look into the underlying mechanics. Although 
they quickly lost interest in the lack of variety and levels, they were deeply fascinated with the 
development because it provided them with a whole new level of complexity, which they found 
intriguing. This gives us an indication that we should bear in mind, that it might provide an excit-
ing experience for our target group to be able to observe the complexity of the system.
Image 12 - Children testing the digital prototype.
==PAGE:97==
88
Prototype Development
•	
In Catch the Light, the children became frustrated when positive feedback appeared on the screen. 
They expressed that it disrupted the game, and did not connect that it was a sign that they com-
pleted the objective. Instead, it was proposed that it would be more sensible with a score, as it 
would also allow for competition against peers.
9.2.4 Limitations
The development and testing of the prototype revealed a number of limitations. During the development, 
a technological issue arose, when the prototype could not be conducted on an iPad. Using the iPad was 
the initial thought, as it had a closer resemblance to the final solution compared to using a computer 
with a separate mouse. Accordingly, the game of Catch the Light became more challenging for the target 
group, given that their fine motor skills and coordination are not as developed as those of an adult, who 
served as the benchmark for the prototype.
The prototype also had the restriction that sound could not be implemented. It would have been valuable 
insight to evaluate if the target group thought it a superior alternative compared to the feedback provided. 
9.3 Alternative Designs
Three alternative design proposals for the solution’s appearance were created, to test the children’s pre-
ferences. It is important to note that the solution has unlimited design opportunities, limited only by the 
imagination. The three design proposals were created to show how the appearance of the solution can dif-
fer in themes, and how these can embrace different features and play opportunities. The design proposals 
were used to gain insight into what would be appealing to the target group.
9.3.1 Description
The three design proposals were suggested appearances based on the same concept, and thus the same 
key features as demonstrated in the other two prototypes. The three suggestions made were; a spaceship, 
a potato, and an apartment building, see figure 32. To support the potential for a play starter, see figure 8, 
each theme included many play possibilities. An opportunity with the apartment building was to create 
shadows in the windows, only visible when turned on. This would enable the product to be also used 
without other game mechanics, and still facilitate simulation play. In regards to the spaceship, an ideal 
opportunity would be to include games space related, such as recreating constellations. Lastly, the potato 
could open up the conversation about science and physics.
==PAGE:98==
Integrate play into the urban environment of Copenhagen
89
Figure 32 - Alternative designs proposals.
9.3.2 Testing
The testing of the three design proposals was performed as a simple A/B test (Martin & Hanington, 2012), 
with the purpose of determining the preferred design. The children were presented with a drawing of each 
of the proposals lined up next to each other, with a brief introduction to each of them. The children were 
then given a ballot, where they could set their vote and add their own proposal, given that they had any 
ideas. 
9.3.3 Results
The following objectives were to be met when testing the possible proposal for the appearance of the 
solution;
1. If one of the design proposals were preferred
2. Input and inspiration for other design proposals
We found that the children preferred the apartment building, compared to the two others, with nine out of 
the sixteen votes. The potato came in second with five votes and lastly, the space theme gathered the last 
two. Besides getting a vote on the preferred design, we had the opportunity to receive the children’s feed-
back and insight into their preferences for the appearance of the solution and the interaction possibilities.
The children’s own design proposals included, among others; a spaceship, a Christmas tree, and cheese, 
see image 14. We noticed that the children were heavily influenced by Christmas being right around the 
corner at the time of testing, hence several of the proposals were Christmas related.
The different themes fostered interesting conversations between the children and us, as facilitators. To-
gether, they began imagining several tales that could be related to the different proposals. This led to new 
ideas of how the design could be further developed and was noted on the ballots for documentation
==PAGE:99==
Prototype Development
90
purposes. One of these ideas was related to the apartment building, where a story was built around how 
the light could be turned on and off at the child’s neighbor’s house when using the solution. Other con-
versations sparked, because of the children’s knowledge of potatoes being able to create electricity. This 
knowledge was gladly explained by the children, who knew of the concept, to their peers.
9.3.4 Limitations
The A/B test of the design proposals was performed after both the digital and physical prototypes had 
been tested. As the time schedule slipped at several rounds of testing, only a limited amount of time was 
left, for the children to create their own proposals. Furthermore, as the children were divided into two se-
parate groups and did not finish at the same time, the last test had to be repeated multiple times, which 
caused disturbance for the children, who had already been told what to do. 
The children were allowed to discuss and question the proposals openly, leaving room for both them and 
us to answer. This, in connection with the children being able to see each other’s answers on the ballot, 
might have led to the children influencing each other. For some of the children, it was a bit too abstract to 
understand that the same solution they had just interacted with, could have a completely different appe-
arance, and that was what they had to vote on. They had a hard time connecting the three solutions, and 
this might have directly affected the outcome of the test.

Image 14 - The childrens proposals for designs.

FINAL SOLUTION
==PAGE:101==
Integrate play into the urban environment of Copenhagen
92
10
Final Solution
The final solution proposal for the Interactive Board has been developed and adapted based on the fin-
dings presented in this report. The findings have manifested in product requirements, and product place-
ment and the results have been obtained through testing.
10.1 Function
The solution function as an interactive communication and game board. The solution is rather simple, as 
the interaction opportunity consists of a number of fields. The fields change when the user interacts with 
them, e.g. by changing colors or turning on or off a light source. The fields are placed orderly allowing the 
user to create pixellated images, patterns, or smaller messages by changing the fields’ characters, encoura-
ging open-ended play.
The solution’s functionality fosters social interaction among both the target group and across genera-
tions. Social interactions arise as the target group is able to interact on the board with others, by either 
competing in games or cooperating to make artwork. The solution invites momentarily play and thereby 
becomes a meeting spot where citizens can interact with each other.
Figure 33 - ’Hello World’ created on the Interactive Board.
In earlier involvement of the target group, the children showed great interest in being creative as they 
were immersed in idea creation. The board assists the target group in thinking more abstractly, by creating 
the pixellated images. It was noticed that this could be challenging, but after a few attempts, they mana-
ged to create rather complex letters, such as Æ, and Ø compared to letters with simpler shapes such as I 
and L. The child’s fine motor skill is also challenged,  which is beneficial for the target group, as this skill is 
still being refined.
==PAGE:102==
As children appreciate feedback through interaction (see section 2.3.7), the interaction with the solution, 
provides the user feedback through a change in the aversion of the fields. The feedback can help increase 
the user experience, as it can assist the user through the interaction, and promote entertainment. Children 
are fascinated by sound and light, and this can be a great tool to include several senses, to make the inte-
raction more inclusive. Furthermore, the solution remains relatively simple, without too many features, to 
both avoid feature creep and maintain a momentary interaction. 
Integrate play into the urban environment of Copenhagen
93
10.1.1 Game Opportunities
The number of fields on the board allows for multiple interaction opportunities, with a greater focus on 
communication and game opportunities. There are many game opportunities - limited only by the ima-
gination. The games should challenge the children’s development, focusing on different skills or create a 
high level of entertainment, containing elements that are appealing to the target group.
We believe that open-ended play can foster unintended play and games. In the dialogue with the peda-
gogues about the concept, they saw several opportunities that had not been considered. One example is, 
having two users compete against each other to turn on and off the light button, which emphasizes that 
the game aspect does not require facilitation or programming. 
10.2 The Design
Requirements have been made to ensure that the board fulfills its purpose and follows the principles of 
inclusive design. It is necessary to have an understanding of materials to be able to develop and build a 
prototype that can handle the outdoor condition for a longer period of time, to test the solution. This inve-
stigation has, however, been out of scope. Through a brief interview with a former Copenhagen Light Festi-
val participant, we received valuable insights on considerations when creating installations for the urban 
space. One of these insights was that polymers that are not biodegradable are solid performers in all kinds 
of weather. It is important that the construction has a drain that allows water to flow out regardless of the 
choice of material, as condensation cannot be avoided. As a result of this, potential electronics should be 
placed at the top of the construction. 
The construction also needs to be robust enough for multiple and various interactions over a longer pe-
riod. A robust construction and design can help prevent vandalism. Additionally, urban spaces that invite 
lingering, provide life and eyes in the neighbourhood that will mitigate the risk of vandalism (København 
Kommune, 2018). 
The shape of the board might vary greatly. However, it is critical that the shape is eye-catching to attract 
the target group. This can be done, by creating a theme for the board’s appearance, see figure 32, which can 
also function as a play starter.
The function of the solution can create attention in the urban space, through created artwork, or included 
features that react to citizens passing. This can create curiosity, especially for the target group (see section 
9.1), aiming for playful interactions.
The board is placed on two feet, lifting it 50 cm above the ground. To accommodate a broader range of
==PAGE:103==
94
citizens, a suggested dimension would be a height of 150 cm, to allow adults to also participate in the in-
teraction see figure 34. The target group are able to reach all the fields as their height is between 120-140 
cm, as the lowest can reach a height of 150 cm with an extensive arm. It was determined from prototype 
testing that a minimum of 50 cm was adequate for two children to interact next to each other. The solution 
should, therefore, not be less than 100 cm wide to allow four children, within the target group, to interact.
Final Solution
Figure 34 - Proposed dimensions for the solution.
The fields have the size 150mm x 150mm, corresponding to a keyboard button. It is crucial that the fields 
are small enough in size to allow the user to create smaller messages to each other. Making the letters, E 
and S, require 5x3 fields, see figure 35.
Figure 35 - Pixellated letters, E and S.  
==PAGE:104==
The size of fields will lead to around 1500 fields on a board of 100 cm x 100 cm. With this amount of fields, 
it is possible to create around 42 letters and unleash creativity in creating images on the board. To make 
fields usable for different users, the fields must be easily accessible and require low physical effort to inte-
ract with (see section 2.3.4). 
The use of buttons will presumably make the interaction intuitive for a broader range of people, as it is 
familiar if the button is simple and embraces tactile recognition (Plotnick, 2018). It is essential that the user 
understand how to interact with the solution immediately, to avoid instructions. However, to assist the 
user in the interaction, simple symbols can be applied, as these are easy for most to interpret and under-
stand, regardless of individuals’ language skills, or concentration level. 
A physical button stimulates the aspect of touch and promotes a sensation of physical feedback (Plotnick, 
2018). Any type of feedback can support the user in the interaction. Feedback, whether sound or tactile 
simulation, can, for instance, function as a confirmation, by a ’click’-sound or smaller vibration. Thus simu-
lating more than one sense, e.g. tactile and auditory, which makes the solution more inclusive.
Integrate play into the urban environment of Copenhagen
95
10.3 Digital Complexity
The final solution includes three distinct design proposals for the concept. Offering an idea of just how 
many various ways this solution could be developed. In our conversation with Kompan, we learned about 
the current opposing trends in play; nature versus digitalization. Where one trend urges the utilization of 
nature, the other urges how to incorporate digitalization in outdoor activities with solutions.
The final solution can encompass both trends depending on the approach for future work. To prove this 
point, we have opted to create three design proposals, each at a different level of complexity; ranging from 
mechanical, and semi-digital to full digital. Each of the proposals is based on the prerequisites previously 
described but with each their own development po-
tential and complications.
10.3.1 Mechanical Solution
The mechanical solution is, in contrast to the other two 
proposals, entirely devoid of digital complexity. This 
implies that the interactive fields, instead of electrical 
components, are flippable fields with different colors 
on each side. This allows for the same opportunity to 
create images or messages. 
The target group of the mechanical solution, as cur-
rently envisioned, would appeal more to the younger 
demographic of our target group.
Figure 36 - Mechanical solution proposal.
==PAGE:105==
10.3.2 Semi-digital Solution
The semi-digital solution is a compromise bet-
ween the mechanical- and the digital proposal. 
The fields are buttons, similar to those of a 
keyboard, that light up when being pushed. As 
the board needs to be connected to power, there 
is an ideal opportunity to incorporate games.
The solution covers the entire target group, as 
it provides a complexity that challenges and 
maintains the children’s interest in recurring in-
teractions. It provides the same opportunities 
as the mechanical solution, which still allows for 
open-ended play.
Final Solution
96
This is mainly because it lacks a level of complexity that the children of the older part of the target group 
desire (see section 8.4.1). To ensure their entertainment at every encounter, they would need to be conti-
nually challenged, which might be hard to achieve without a technical aspect in the solution. 
The design’s simplicity means that facilitation could be necessary to enable various play interactions. This 
could affect the children’s interest in the artifact, as aspects of open-ended play might seem less noticeable, 
in contrast to already established games. As a result, it might be necessary to help facilitate certain games, 
which could be done by having accompanying guidelines. However, the observations showed, that the 
children do not necessarily read these guidelines, which might prove a problem.
Pattern recognition, a game principle tested with the prototype (se section 9.2), would be possible to 
facilitate through this solution. Users would be able to jointly challenge each other, thus creating an exer-
cise that covers all the developmental skills; motor, cognitive, and social. The absence of too many game 
features may, however, also prove beneficial as it brings attention to the solution’s core function, com-
munity building through drawings and notes. By not having game mechanics, the solution might end 
up with something resembling an art installation, that can support the children in leaving their mark on 
their environment. Therefore, the mechanical solution solves the absolutely essential need that has been 
identified, the need for fostering a sense of community in the cityscape. Aside from potential vandalism, 
it would require little to no upkeep, and the installation would be rather simple, as it would not require 
power to function. 
The primary issue with the solution is that it might be challenging to maintain the target group’s interest, 
especially over a longer period of time, as there is a chance that it won’t offer enough excitement or play 
opportunities. In general, the solution provides 
more sedentary activities and would require ad-
ditional facilitating features, such as guidelines 
to provide examples of play activities.
Figure 37 - Semi-digital solution proposal.
==PAGE:106==
Integrate play into the urban environment of Copenhagen
97
In contrast to the mechanical proposal, the semi-digital allows for the possibility of facilitating different 
games. One of the solution’s key features is light, which is not only beneficial for entertainment but can 
also prove beneficial to the general urban environment. Light not only draws attention and makes the so-
lution exciting, but it is also well-recognized that light has a direct impact on the population’s perception 
of security. 
The semi-digital solution is ideal for games such as whack-a-mole, because of the design of the buttons 
and light features. Implementing this game would, however, require the right feedback. This feedback 
could be various different options, such as using light or sound. Using sound would be ideal, as it would 
provide instant feedback on how well the children do in regard to reaching the objective, but this feature 
could also be done with either blinking lights or a change in color. It is also important to keep in mind, that 
we focus on creating momentarily play, where implementing a high score may increase the competitive 
aspect, but it will also lengthen the game’s amusement. Without feedback, such as a high score, the chil-
dren will be limited to utilizing their own logical thinking and cognitive abilities, which could benefit their 
development.
The semi-digital solution holds several benefits, as explained in the other proposals. One of the main be-
nefits of this solution is that it is able to facilitate the game. This was mentioned by YOLI as one of the main 
findings from their product development, as this was a huge motivator for play seen in their investigations.
Maintenance is one of the primary issues with the solution. Given its electric components, it would com-
paratively need to be kept, to ensure that the lights work and it doesn’t impose a risk if broken. Another 
challenge would be the need for the implementation of other games when the children have a change in 
interest as a result of trends changing.
10.3.3 Digital Solution 
In the digital solution, the fields are touchscreen, 
which is operated by touching the screen. The 
fields are, however, still shaped similarly to the 
previous alternatives, which creates the appe-
arance of a large pixel. This signifies that several 
options in regard to color change are available in 
this solution. 
As the target group requires to be challenged and 
enjoys elements of unpredictability, the solution 
could satisfy their needs and maintain their inte-
rest. Additionally, it was found that children have 
a preference of digital play (Lego, 2018), which can 
be deemed a beneficial factor. 
Figure 36 - Digital solution proposal.
==PAGE:107==
Final Solution
98
The solution allows for both customization and experimentation with feedback and other features, pro-
viding a range of versatile communication options for the target group. Additionally, there is the poten-
tial of saving the messages written on the boards as data, which can allow for the solution to be used as 
a communal wishing well. This could allow stakeholders to ask questions to their community and then 
gather the data, allowing for interaction between, e.g. the children and adults in a neighborhood and the 
municipality.
The solution offers countless options for incorporating games over time and the flexibility of evolving the 
games through various themes and visual styles, thus practically offering endless play opportunities. And 
with its similarities to other tech solutions that the children are already familiar with, it has a higher chance 
of capturing the children’s initial interest compared to the other solution proposals. 
There are several concerns with the solution that would have to be considered and dealt with, for it to fulfill 
its purpose. One of these issues may be the parents’ apprehension about letting their children play with a 
computer outside, as a general concern is that children spend too much time looking at screens (Appendix 
03.2). Another key issue is the requirement of upkeep and the likelihood of being exposed to vandalism. 
Our general observations are that objects of higher technology are more likely to be exposed to vanda-
lism, comparatively, e.g. the newly installed digital bus stops.
Lastly, the fully digital solution proposal is assumed to be more fragile compared to the other proposals 
and would require more maintenance. This is deemed a risk, as children tend to be unforgiving, resulting 
in a malfunction directly influencing the children’s perception of the solution, thus creating hesitation to 
interact at another time.
All three proposals have the potential to solve the final problem, and the final decision thus depends on 
preference and placement. A common aspect in all three design proposals is that a variety of game oppor-
tunities could be implemented, and the required maintenance depends on the digital complexity. 
10.4 Expected Outcome
We find that implementing the solution, no matter its level of digital complexity, can provide important 
advantages to the cityscape. First, it allows the children the opportunity to leave their mark on their neigh-
borhood, which can lead to an increase in their autonomy and, as a result, a greater sense of independence 
when roaming in the city. The Interactive Board furthermore, has the potential to serve as a meeting place, 
where children can meet up with one another in their immediate environment, in a similar fashion as the 
playground. By bringing the play to the street, there is an opportunity for it to happen spontaneously as 
opposed to having to be planned (see section 2.3). We assume that there is a possibility, that the children 
might be intrigued or convinced to play outside if they can spot their peers playing on the street in their 
neighborhoods from their homes.
Long-term, it is anticipated that the solution could contribute to promoting more play on the curbside. By 
implementing play opportunities, there is a chance to influence the current play culture in the city. Using 
the behavioral change method proposed by Cash et. al (2017), the artifact aims to intervene directly in 
==PAGE:108==
the behavior, hoping to reach the implicit system, through the use of informational strategies, focused on 
creating awareness and changing norms and attitudes of playing on the curbside (see section 2.3.8). 
The behavioral change in children’s play in the street might then influence the play culture for a positive 
change. Furthermore, a rise in activity on the streets increases the likelihood that competing interests, 
such as traffic, might be forced to be reduced in the living areas of the city. Considering traffic is one of the 
major concerns of the parents, a reduction would most likely lead to the parents beginning to have a gre-
ater sense of safety in regard to their children and their mobility in the city (see section 4.2). Implementing 
more play on the curbside, will not only be beneficial to children but will also benefit the community as a 
whole since if the environment is safe for children, it is safe for everyone (Rogala & Stokols, 2015).
Integrate play into the urban environment of Copenhagen
99
