IMPLENTATION PLAN
==PAGE:110==
101
Integrate play into the urban environment of Copenhagen
11
Implementation Plan
This section outlines the specific steps needed to be considered for the implementation of the final solu-
tion proposal. The implementation plan takes into consideration factors, such as placement and potential 
barriers. Lastly, a plan for future work has been considered.
11.1 Implementation Plan
To clarify the precise locations the proposed solutions could be applied, an implementation plan was de-
veloped. Through the development of this plan, we discovered that the preferred location of placement 
is significantly impacted by the chosen proposal. Some of the proposals’ ideal locations might make it dif-
ficult to achieve the ultimate purpose, which is to encourage a change in play culture in neighborhoods, 
by promoting play on the curbside. Amongst others, we found that the implementation of the digital so-
lution is most viable in areas of more technological advancement. This solution is also perceived as more 
fragile in the exposure of long-term use, and outdoor conditions, compared to the other two proposals, 
thus an aspect that needs to be considered.
 
Additionally, there are certain potential barriers and risks that need to be considered for the successful 
execution of the solution, which is being taken into account through this implementation plan.
Figure 37 - Solution proposal placement at Indre Nørrebro.
==PAGE:111==
102
Placement of the Mechanical Proposal
Similarly to the semi-digital proposal, the mechanical one can be implemented almost anywhere in the 
cityscape, as it has the benefit of not requiring power. However, given that it provides no light, it is at a 
higher risk of being vandalized, meaning that it would benefit from being placed in a visible, lit-up area. 
The solution would be very suitable in environments of nature, as well as in areas that younger children 
frequent with their parents. Excellent options for placement would therefore be areas such as Møllegade 
and ByOasen at Nørrebro, because of the closer proximity to nurseries, kindergartens, and schools.
 
Besides the digital complexity defined, the solution still has the possibility to be customized for the given 
setting that it is implemented. With the basic elements being the same, the overall appearance is adapta-
ble to the intended environment.  
Placement
Considering potential locations for the solution is crucial when discussing implementation. Common to all 
three solution proposals is that they need to be located in the curbside zones, see figure 37. Therefore the 
solution must be situated along buildings, to ensure that the traffic has the best possible overview of the 
citizens interacting. Furthermore, it is evident that all proposals must be implemented in locations where 
the curbside is wider, and where the traffic is lighter. This is to guarantee that the solution will not put the 
children at risk while playing. As two of the proposed solutions require power sources to perform properly, 
this is also a necessary factor to consider in deciding what areas were deemed suitable for the solution.
Placement of the Digital Proposal
We see a possibility for the digital solution proposal to be implemented in more technologically advanced 
and metropolitan regions, which would mean that it is moved a bit from the neighborhoods, where the 
solution was initially thought to be placed. With the final solution, we aim to implement it in settings bet-
ween schools and residential areas, however, this proposal might need to be placed closer to schools, to 
avoid vandalism. Areas we find that the solution would fit in, include areas such as close to Nørrebroparken 
by Jagtvej, because of nearby schools and extracurricular activities. However, it would need to be further 
explored and investigated, whether children independently walk around in these areas, which are often 
busier than the neighborhoods we targeted in our research.
Placement of the Semi-digital Proposal
The semi-digital solution proposal is assessed to be suitable in most areas. A good opportunity would be 
to implement it in areas where we have done our observations, such as the residential areas of Nørrebro, as 
they are close to schools and other institutions. Because of the proximity to the schools, there is less busy 
traffic leading up to wider pavements.
Implementation Plan
==PAGE:112==
103
11.2 Potential barriers
It is crucial to assess potential risks and barriers that could be present in connection with the implementa-
tion of the proposed solution in the urban environment. In the assessment of the feasibility of the imple-
mentation of the solution, it should be determined what reservations need to be made and what factors
 should be further investigated for a successful implementation.
The following risks have been identified in the investigation of the implementation of the proposed 
solution:
Maintenance
The amount of maintenance required to keep the solution operating needs to be taken into 
account. Therefore, it is crucial to consider the proposals’ construction, materials, usage, and 
durability.
Acceptance
The children’s independent interactions with the solution are granted on the basis of the pa-
rent’s permission. This implies that the placement of the product should be carefully planned 
to fit the areas where children are allowed to roam independently, which can be observed as 
the places with the heaviest activity on the curbside of children. We have observed that these areas typi-
cally are in and around schools and leisure-time institutions. 
Culture Change
It is necessary to expect that the implementation of the proposed solution will not lead to a 
direct change in the play culture. Changing a play culture in the city would require a change 
in behavioral habits, they both need time and effort. The solution should be perceived as one 
of many tools to help the children become more comfortable with claiming the city as their own. Thus 
encouraging them to ”dare” interact with the solution implemented.
 
Disturbance
For the solution to meet the criterion of the product requirement, it is important that the so-
lution is not a nuisance to other people. This emphasizes that the solution is required to not 
cause other citizens to feel restrained in their behaviors in the city, or cause unnecessary anno-
yance. It is crucial to take into account if the location of the solution can result in an excessive amount of 
traffic on the curbside. 
Safety
The issue of safety is one of the major barriers to the solution’s implementation on the curbside. 
The solution needs to adhere to regulations set by Dansk Standard to be allowed implementa-
tion in the public space. Despite safety being a factor that has been black-boxed in the scope 
of the project, it is crucial to mention, because of its significant impact on the ability for implementation. 
 
Integrate play into the urban environment of Copenhagen
==PAGE:113==
Implementation Plan
104
Traffic
Implementation of the solution is impeded by the surrounding infrastructure and the amount 
of traffic. This ties into the safety issue, with parents’ concerns about their children’s movement 
in the city, is mostly attributed to traffic (see section 4.2). For the children’s safety, parents pro-
hibit their children from playing in traffic-dense areas. As a result, it is important to consider the volume of 
traffic in the region where the solution is intended to be implemented. When referring to traffic, it implies 
both cyclists and cars.
Black Boxed Areas
In this project, we have chosen to blackbox elements of the implementation plan, due to a lack of time and 
resources. Some of the elements that have been disregarded, is among other things, the choice of mate-
rials and a financial implementation plan. These two aspects have, although important, been assessed as 
being more relevant in future work.
11.3 Future Work
The project has been scoped to ensure to completion of 
the defined objectives within the given time frame. Cer-
tain tasks have been out of scope, but are crucial for the 
completion of a successful project, thus recommend to 
be done as future work. This section provides recommen-
dations for concrete goals to be achieved in the next six 
months. This includes an investigation of the design spe-
cifications of the solution and development possibilities, 
such as testing interactions, feedback, and material opti-
ons. These investigations are conducted in order to make 
well-considered and informed decisions. To ensure that 
the final solution can be implemented successfully, these 
concrete tasks are essential to perform. 
Test of Functionality and Game 
Principles
The immediate task would be to test the 
functionality of the prototype. This includes 
testing a variety of buttons to decide which one works 
ideally with the purpose of the solution. By inviting a 
focus group, that represents the target group, it would 
be possible to determine both the functionality and what 
game principle, should be chosen and developed into 
the solution for further testing. When testing the game 
principles, it is necessary to create a prototype that allows 
for more than one user to interact with the solution at a 
time,
Figure 38 - Roadmap for the next six months.
==PAGE:114==
Build a To-Scale Prototype
The next stage would be to create a to-scale prototype of the semi-digital design proposal, 
as it best simulates the functionality of both ends of the digital complexity. The prototype 
would be built with the results previously gathered in mind. Final decisions in regard to the 
prototype’s size, height, design, and appearance would need to be made. The construction 
and material choice are important to consider when building the prototype, as the solution is to be tested 
outside over a longer period of time. 
105
to evaluate if the entertainment value is related to social interaction, as related to social interaction, as is 
assumed. 
Placement Considerations
The ideal placement for the solution can be found through the use of Gehl’s methods 
(Gehl Playscapes, n.d.). It should be kept in mind, that the solution is to be placed on the 
curbside, and to be placed between residential areas and schools. Once the ideal locati-
on has been determined, authorization of installation needs to be requested for legal 
implementation. Both the municipality and private citizens would be possible to contact in this regard. 
It would be beneficial to monitor and evaluate whether the positioning in the given location affects play, 
if some aspects of the solution should be more apparent, and if the surrounding interior, such as benches, 
has an impact.
Test of Interactions
We wish to test if the prototype functions in the intended space and if the target group and 
their interactions align with our expectations. Testing of the prototype can provide useful 
insights, and a better understanding of the use of the solution in the urban space, thus hel-
ping to eliminate pitfalls.
We want to explore the interactions through two different approaches. Firstly, we want to test it through 
direct facilitation. This would allow us to introduce the solution and assist them through its use. This would 
be ideal for gathering data and insights, both by observing up close and by asking questions. 
Secondly, we would want the solution implemented in the space over a longer period of time, approxi-
mately three-plus weeks, in a location that matches the placement requirements. This would allow for the 
solution to be observed in its natural environment and allow for the collection of unbiased data, as the 
behaviors could be observed. Data collection of the interactions, such as those described in the digital 
solution proposal (see section 10.3.3), should be considered to gain an understanding of the interactions 
happening when no one is observing.
Detailing
Lastly, it would be advantageous to test the detailing of the product, which includes the 
functionality design, game mechanics, and overall appearance. It would be beneficial to 
build multiple prototypes to test factors, such as the size, shape, texture, and feedback of 
the solution.

Outro
12.1 Discussion
The final solution has been greatly impacted by a number of decisions made during the design process. 
Some of these choices were decided before the project’s official beginning, one was to focus on devel-
oping an artifact that would encourage play in Copenhagen outside of playgrounds. Alternative strategies 
for changing prevailing behaviors could have included removing the boundaries surrounding playgrounds 
or looking into modifying the traffic patterns in the vicinity of play areas. Instead of focusing on solving a 
possible symptom, we set out to develop a solution to address the root cause.
The most significant decisions and influences on the design process are to be covered in the following 
sections:
Partnership with Gehl
In connection with our partnership with Gehl Architects, there have been some very clear controversies 
from the start, when working with urban spaces. One of these controversies has, among other things, been 
traffic. In Gehl’s ideologies, traffic is seen as the antithesis of a functioning and present city for community 
development and pedestrians.
Had we chosen to focus on traffic and infrastructure in this project, it would have been ideal to work on 
changing traffic behavior to achieve more play in the streets.
108
Integrate play into the urban environment of Copenhagen
Intervention Area
A bottom-up approach was used in the development of the final solution. This has greatly affected our 
choice of intervention within the selected scope. We chose to focus on the intervention on the curbside, as 
we saw the potential for implementing a tangible change. If the process, had followed a top-down appro-
ach, it would have been ideal to create an intervention at the city scale and work directly with Copenhagen 
Municipality. The project would then have required changing and developing of new strategies rather 
than developing a tangible solution for direct implementation on the curbside. 
The choice of the curbside was decided for a number of reasons, including that play has previously taken 
place there, children move about independently on their commutes, and it abuts residential buildings, 
where 70% of public engagement occurs (København Kommune, 2018). Given that children typically do not 
have much independent free time to play throughout the day, the last two points are especially important. 
As a result, we believed it crucial to implement the solution in the space the children already reside. This 
creates the opportunity to play without having to commute to parks or playgrounds; instead, they have to 
option on their daily commutes in the city. It is presumed that providing playful interactions on the curb-
side can have an impact on the spontaneity of play. Furthermore, Copenhagen Municipality has already 
placed a high priority on developing parks and squares, as they are deemed vital community hubs for all 
generations. 
If parks and squares had been chosen in favor of the curbside, one of the key aspects to look into would 
how to attract children to visit these parts of the urban space. 
==PAGE:118==
A more spacious available area would consequently allow for a variety of different solutions possibilities. 
Additionally, parks and squares are generally void of traffic, which presents a completely different safety 
aspect compared to the curbside. 
By focusing on implementing the solution on the curbside, we saw an opportunity to shed light on the 
problem of the absence of play and impose a direct influence on the culture, providing an opportunity for 
the change that is needed. Moreover, it offers a chance to promote open and visible play. 
Had the placement been on heavily trafficked roads, it would have been an obvious opportunity to work 
on solutions that aimed to avoid accidents and promote traffic safety, to the extent mentioned in the in-
terview with Camilla from YOLI.
Country Dependent Culture
Copenhagen, as the selected region for the scope, has also had a significant role in the outcome of the 
project. The overall attitude towards children and safety in Copenhagen differs significantly from that of 
many other places. For instance, it is typical to leave babies in strollers outside of cafés unattended. The 
bicycle traffic is also vastly different from that of other cultures, meaning that children are exposed to it a 
lot sooner and become a part of the traffic at an early age as a result. One of the key findings we found was 
that it was shocking to others with different cultural backgrounds, how early children commute indepen-
dently to and from school. But even within Denmark, the play culture is immensely different, travelling 30 
minutes outside of Copenhagen it is common for children to play on the residential streets. As noted by 
Kompan, this does not currently occur in the metropolitan area, hence our focus on bringing it back.
If the solution would be implemented elsewhere, in other cities, regions, or countries, the solution would 
require tailoring to the environment in which it is to be used. As a result, a thorough examination of the 
setting would be required, taking into consideration factors such as culture, traffic, and general behavior, 
to prevent the failure of the solution. It is possible that the investigated area does not require the same 
solution, given that they might not share the same issues. 
Target Group and User Involvement
In the design process, we got the opportunity to incorporate a fourth-grade class from Skolen Ved Søerne 
at Frederiksberg. They had a huge influence on the outcome of the final solution, as they helped us obtain 
an understanding of our target group, as well as aided us in the design and testing of prototypes. They 
represent the elder segment of the target group, as the class consisted of students between the ages of 
nine and ten. Obviously, they cannot represent the entire target group. The children residing in the neig-
hborhood of Frederiksberg is another consideration to take into account, as naturally, there is a cultural 
variation that is different between the boroughs. Additionally, it is important to keep in mind that the chil-
dren’s identities and interests are greatly influenced by the class culture, meaning that if we had spoken to 
the parallel class, the outcome might have been completely different.
Besides the impact of the location, the time of year of our conducted investigations also influenced the
109
Integrate play into the urban environment of Copenhagen
==PAGE:119==
outcome. We discovered in the literature that general outdoor activities usually drop in the fall and winter, 
directly affecting play behavior and general traffic.
Outro
Parental Influence
Understanding how to shape the culture and behavior surrounding play in the city has posed a significant 
challenge. Our research has revealed that children are a sensitive topic for parents, with numerous rules 
and opinions about child-rearing creating substantial pressure for them. Comprehending the concerns 
of parents and the factors that impact their influence on children and play behavior could be a subject 
worthy of its own study. In this project, we had no direct contact with parents we, therefore, view them 
as silent stakeholders. Instead, we have relied on literature, accounts from pedagogues, and our observa-
tions. Because of the complexity of humans, and the amount of anthropological research that would be 
required, it has been challenging to fully comprehend parenting styles and child-related behavior in such 
a limited period of time. However, as a great deal of societal pressure and uncertainty among parents have 
been observed, we can assume that we might not have received honest responses on the subject of chil-
dren if they were to be interviewed. We do not consider this a great loss because as opposed to directly 
including the parents, we opted for other approaches, like observations, to analyze their behaviors. How-
ever, due to their significant influence on the project, would be considered vital to include the parents in 
the long term.
Inclusivity Principles
Throughout the course of the project, we have sought to incorporate principles of inclusivity to enga-
ge the target audience. However, in working with a diverse range of individuals, it is acknowledged that 
complete attainment of this objective may not be possible. This is evident in our persona, which is a gene-
ralized depiction of the children and their development in the designated age span. Targeting too many 
individuals at once creates the risk of missing all, resulting in the incompletion of the actual objective. This 
is demonstrated by the strategies imposed by the Copenhagen Municipality that aims to benefit everyone 
but as they admit, have no real impact on anyone. Therefore, it is preferable to concentrate on a minority 
and then meet their requirements, as doing so can provide the opportunity for a variety of users to still be 
able to have valuable interactions.
In short, there are many ways the project could have been approached, leading to numerous ways it could 
have been solved. During the project, we had to iterate multiple times when new knowledge or insights 
were discovered. All choices made have been at the expense of other choices, thus emphasizing, that the 
solution is completely dependent on our approach to the problem. The final solution, with the three de-
sign proposals, should be seen as a stepping stone towards the development of an artifact that is set out 
to change the play culture in Copenhagen.
110
==PAGE:120==
12.2 Conclusion
As a result of the city’s development, in the last couple of decades, play has been relocated off the stre-
et and onto playgrounds spread across the cityscape. This is an inconvenient development, given that 
both parents’ and children’s everyday life are increasingly getting busier. When play is then designated 
for certain locations, the children might be deprived of the opportunity to engage in independent play. 
This is an issue, as play has a direct impact on children’s development. Play eventually becomes limited to 
playgrounds, parks, and the confinement of the house. The common denominator of these venues is the 
parents’ presence. 
In addition to being important for the child’s development, play is also crucial for the perception of the city. 
Children playing on the street fosters a sense of security and helps create a common ground for citizens to 
meet, thus helping build a thriving community. These two elements are essential for developing a functi-
oning city for people. Increasing enjoyment on the street is viewed as an opportunity to foster a general 
feeling of well-being amongst the cities entire population. 
The project went through a bottom-up approach to solve the matter of creating an intervention that ai-
med to contribute to the change of play culture in Copenhagen. The proposed solution was an artifact 
installed on the curbside that sought to encourage children to momentarily play interactions on their daily 
commutes. These interactions are assumed to help alter the behaviors of the users, incorporating more 
independent play for children in their everyday life.
The specific target group was chosen on the basis, that this is when the children begin to move indepen-
dently in the city, on their daily routes. What was discovered was that concurrently this is the time when 
they attend numerous leisure activities, and, as a result, do not have much spare time to play on their own 
terms.
An important discovery, that influenced the project greatly was that the solution had to accommodate 
social interactions. Given the social interactions’ significant impact on the children’s interest in play in these 
formative years. Therefore, if the solution is to be applied in an urban setting, it is absolutely vital that it can 
accommodate these interactions.
With an urban space that is not designed for children, we saw that it was necessary to create a solution for 
them and their play, and therefore came up with the following problem statement: How can we encourage 
momentarily playful interactions for children between the age of seven to ten on the curbside in the urban en-
vironment of Copenhagen?
The solution has been developed with best practices in mind, with the problem being solvable through 
a variety of efforts. Through evaluation and testing of the concepts developed, we chose the best-suited 
option, which was the Interactive Board. This was assessed based on factors such as the target group’s 
interest, product requirement, product placement, and project scope. The interactive board aims to en-
courage play and social interactions, but its most essential feature is its ability to enable children to leave 
their imprint on the city. This ability can foster sentiments of inclusion, which can lead to autonomy in the
Integrate play into the urban environment of Copenhagen
111
==PAGE:121==
Outro
112
city and feelings of independence. Given that children are capable of adapting and utilizing their surroun-
dings in imaginative ways when provided adequate space, the core challenge is not limited to a specific 
solution.
As we discovered that children do not have enough leisure time, it was important that the proposed solu-
tions facilitate brief, yet meaningful play experiences. Furthermore, this was relevant given the location of 
the planned implementation, the curbside. As this space is currently deemed unsafe, it would be difficult 
to convince parents to let their children interact for longer periods of time.
We want to foster momentary play, five to ten minutes of interaction, through the implementation of the 
solution on the curbside. The solution is to be placed in an area of high traffic of children, such as between 
residential areas and schools. We anticipate that by minimizing the features offered, the interactions can 
be balanced to be within the established five to ten minutes. These features include feedback, level of dif-
ficulty, and complexity.
By developing a solution around universal and inclusive design principles, an attempt has been made to 
create a solution that accommodates beyond the target group. The selected target group does not narrow 
the reach of the solution. On the contrary, it widens it, as children don’t fit the average standard in terms of 
physical dimensions or level of development. Three distinct solution proposals were created to ensure that 
the solution could be implemented homogeneously in the urban environment. These suggested solutions 
offer the potential for various aesthetics and functions, in addition to various degrees of complexity.
Before the implementation of the final solution on the curbside, it needs to be further developed and te-
sted. Function, feedback, and final appearance are some of the work that needs to be done. It should also 
be investigated whether the target group can be nudged into interaction with the solution in the public 
domain. After this work, the goal is that the solution can be provided as an opportunity for a change in the 
play culture on the curbside in Copenhagen.
12.3 Reflection
The project’s focus underwent several modifications as a result of the iterative process of gathering and 
analyzing information. The complexity of play culture became apparent during the design process, which 
prompted the choice of developing an intervention through a solution proposal for the curbside, with the 
aim of creating change within the system.The process of design would have been streamlined if the curb-
side had been the original focus of the project. 
The project’s direction has shifted multiple times as a result of the iterative nature of the process. Implying 
that we initially focused on issues such as children’s lack of independent play in cities to arrive at incorpo-
rating and encouraging playful interactions on the curbside level for children to play on their own terms. 
Thus hoping to potentially alter the play culture identified and ensure a return of play to the curbside. 
Our goal was to gain a comprehensive understanding of various aspects pertaining to children and their 
play in urban environments. To accomplish this objective, we adopted a multi-faceted approach that en-
compassed desk research, interviews, observations, behavioral mapping, and co-creation. 
==PAGE:122==
Thus getting an understanding of the behaviors observed in the city and their relation to the culture, the 
city, and the children’s development. To evaluate concepts and ideas, we used methods such as MVP pro-
totype testing and concept evaluation with the stakeholders.
During the process, we encountered challenges with the execution of some methods, which consequently 
led to unexpected or a lack of outcomes. 
We discovered that using the MVP method allowed us to quickly establish solutions ready for testing, 
however, the children were not able to comprehend the solution, given that they were too abstract for 
their level of logical thinking. This prevented the children from interacting with the solution, as they were 
neither able to interpret the use nor understand the context. Naturally, we made other discoveries while 
observing the interactions and the lack thereof, but it would have been intriguing to discover the possible 
interactions that the children might have with the prototype.
The approach to the selection of stakeholders could have looked vastly different with another take on the 
project. We were especially fascinated by the psychology and research done on play and had the oppor-
tunity to get in contact with experts. However, we decided to concentrate on play as an integrated part of 
the cityscape, meaning that our focus was on urban environmental development. If we had changed our 
focus, we might have had the opportunity to create solutions that more specifically targeted the children’s 
development of motor, cognitive, and social skills. We opted for involving the children instead, which led 
to the acquirement of ideations and feedback, as opposed to relying on the theory and assumptions of 
scholars.
The project planning involved a high level of optimism regarding the expected outcomes and the devel-
opment of a comprehensive solution.  We had high ambitions for the outcome and development of a final 
solution, as well as striving to involve the target group in the process. This required extensive planning 
of co-creation workshops and prototype testing. This may have led to occasionally hasty decisions being 
made, without considering important factors such as the children’s perception of the world. 
Fortunately, we chose an MVP approach, which allowed us to fail fast and learn quickly. Had less time been 
spent on determining the ultimate scope, more time might have been available for the development of a 
solution. Instead, the final outcome led to three proposals at different levels of complexity with an under-
standing of potential implementation possibilities.
The partnership with Gehl Architects greatly influenced the project, the methodologies employed, and 
the way the city has been examined and perceived as a system. Throughout the whole project, the colla-
boration has been effective and incredibly helpful, as we got full access to their tools and resources as a 
result. Given that the Gehl ideologies and theories were used in the construction of the project; it can be 
presumed that the scope would have been vastly different if in partnership with another actor. Lastly, the 
project has been greatly influenced by the viewpoints of Copenhagen Municipalities landscape architects, 
the play specialists from YOLI and Kompan, and the pedagogues from Skolen ved Søerne, whose ideas and 
methods of operation have a bearing on how the project has been perceived.

on each of the three 
questions - there is no 
limit to how many 
post-its notes the 
participant can place in
 
each question. They are 
allowed to write or draw 
on the notes.

10 min 
Keep up the energy level 
and motivation for the 
next part of the workshop. 
Input: Participants are
 
low on energy.
Output: Energy level
 
up.

participants leave the 
classroom to prepare
 
for the next activity.

==PAGE:201==
Co-creation workshop
Co-creation workshop 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
         
Appendix no. 05.1
Contact us: stewartsof@gmail.com or 53 34 33 73
Co-creation 
workshop with children
Target group
Time frame
4th grade at Skolen ved Søerne, Frederiks-
berg.  There are around 20-25 children in the 
class in the age range of 9 to 11 years old, and 
we expect that all who are present will 
participate in the workshop.
The co-creation workshop will be done in the 
lecture slot of supportive teaching (Understøt-
tende Undervisning), which lasts two times 
45 minutes in the timeframe 12.25-13.55 on 
a Thursday in late October or early November.
This workshop is conducted as a part of our Master’s Thesis at DTU, which is about integrating play into the city. The purpose 
of the workshop is to include children and their opinions in the development process of our project. The children will be 
included in creative activities that aim to support them children in the ideation process of their ideal elements of and for 
play.
Procedure
Materials
Materials include; playdough, crayons, paper, 
post-its, cardboard, pipe cleaners and more.
Expected outcome
Requirements
The outcome of the workshop is expected to be a large amount of 
qualitative data on the children's thoughts and models that can support 
our own ideation process. We expect a minimum of one model per 
participating child but have no requirements about its execution or the 
scale of it. Furthermore, we expect to gather the children's thoughts on 
what play is and what they most enjoy about play. 
We would like parental permission to collect data and take pictures of 
the workshop. It is required that at least one teacher or pedagogue is 
present while we conduct the workshop to ensure that the children feel 
comfortable and safe. We require that a classroom is available, with 
tables and chairs for all of the children and that the necessary technical 
equipment for presenting a PowerPoint is available.
